(Meat Shed) Oct., 1906 REPORT OF MEDICAL OFFICER OF HEALTH FOR THE CITY OF LONDON. 1907 \%\ 20 MAY US ANNUAL REPORT^ GIF <THHF*^EDICAL OFFICER OF HEALTH YEAR 1907, To the Right Honourable the Lord Mayor, Aldermen and Commons of the Corporation of London. Public Health Department, Guildhall, E.C., 24th March, 1908. Gentlemen, In conformity with the requirements of the Local Government Board, I have the honour to present my eighth Annual Report as Medical Officer of Health for the City of London. In reviewing the work of sanitary administration during 1907 and previous years, it has been interesting to note the gradual evolution of the attitude of the citizens towards progressive hygiene. The public have by slow degrees learnt to recognise the value of practical sanitary science, and this growing knowledge has tended to increase the responsibility attaching to those whose duty it is to carry out the obligations imposed by the ever-increasing statutory requirements which come into being, pari passu, with the spread of education. Ministers of State and the Heads of Government Departments responsible for the health and well-being of the community are at the present time taking an active interest in all that relates to the subject, and many local authorities are finding that if the work is to be performed efficiently, their staffs of officials are inadequate to cope with the fresh duties cast upon them. The various sections of this report dealing with the administration of the Sale of Food and Drugs Acts, the Factory and Workshop Acts, the steps taken for preventing the spread of infectious diseases, the control of food, supplies, &c., illustrate the wide range of subjects included in State hygiene. Year by year some fresh matter for special supervision is added to the list, the two latest being in the direction of the reduction of infantile mortality, and the medical inspection of school children. As an illustration of the change that has taken place in public opinion with regard to personal hygiene, it may be mentioned that last year a Bill 10 was introduced in the Commons for, inter alia, granting statutory powers (in certain circumstances) for the forcible detention and compulsory personal cleansing of individuals, without other warrant than the Act. Not only was such a measure promoted, but it became law, and is now on the Statute Book.* Only a few years ago the idea of advocating legislation of this character, which certainly interferes with the liberty of the subject, would have been deemed preposterous. It is evident, therefore, that the crusade against filth and disease is by no means complete, and even now the Local Government Board are engaged in framing regulations for a wider control of food stuffs under the powers conferred upon them last year by the Public Health (Regulation as to Food) Act. These will probably be of a far-reaching character, and further enlarge the duties and responsibilities of health authorities. The day is probably not far distant when the legislature will see the advisability, in the interest of the honest trader and of the public, of passing a " Pure Food Law" such as now obtains in the United States. Under this measure, manufacturers of drugs, tinned and potted foods, &c., are obliged to affix labels telling the exact truth about their contents. Thus one American, firm it is stated, now labels its "Best Strawberry Jam" as containing:—"Apple Juice 45 per cent., Corn Syrup 48 per cent., Granulated Sugar 6 per cent, and Colouring Matter and Flavour 1 per cent." The purchaser of this compound will have no difficulty in noting that it contains no strawberry at all. Another important provision is that when a preservative has been added, the nature and amount must be declared. The public have a right to know what they are eating. The maxim "caveat emptor" may be good from a lawyer's standpoint, but the sanitarian looks upon it from a different point of view, and the sooner the public in this country are protected from the iniquities of the unscrupulous trader, the better it will be. In earlier days the time of a sanitary inspector was chiefly occupied in looking after small property, clustered together in courts and alleys, in which the City at one time abounded. It is well known that this class of property is now practically non-existent in the City, and at first sight it might appear that, as a consequence, there would be less work for these officers. As a matter of fact, directly the contrary obtains. It is obvious that the defects that more generally require attention relate to sanitary conveniences. Now in the tenement class property that formerly existed, these places were generally placed in the yards, and frequently used in common. These were readily accessible to the Inspector, and it did not then take much time for him to examine the sanitary accommodation provided for * These powers are in operation in the Metropolis with regard to the cleansing of inmates of common lodging-houses and children attending public elementary schools. (2 Edw. 7 cap. clxxv., sec. 37). 11 a whole row of houses. The very large buildings containing offices that now, in most cases, occupy the sites of these old tenement houses, take up far more time in inspection than the premises they have displaced, because, as a rule, they have a separate block of sanitary conveniences on every floor, each of which has to be carefully examined at the request of a class of people far more exacting than that which occupied the old houses. It is true that many of these places are fitted with lifts, but nevertheless the work is laborious, entailing as it does, so much going up and down stairs. It frequently happens that an Inspector may have a dozen large buildings in hand at once where the entire system of drainage of each is being reconstructed, no portion of which is allowed to be covered up until he has inspected and tested it in sections. He has also to exercise a careful supervision in each case in order to secure that the works in progress are conducted strictly in accordance with the plans deposited at this office, from which no variations are allowed without the sanction of the Medical Officer of Health. A Sanitary Inspector in the City may be therefore almost considered to be acting as a Clerk of the Works on a large scale, but with greater responsibilities. His duties are such that it is not infrequent for his views to clash with those of property owners, contractors and architects, and it is clear that not only must he have an intimate knowledge of his profession—which can only be acquired by an extensive practical experience—but he must possess much tact and discrimination to ensure that the Corporation requirements are fulfilled in their entirety. It is highly satisfactory to record the fact that no complaints are ever received as to the manner in which these duties are performed in the City of London, and further, that it is a rare occurrence to be compelled to take proceedings for non-compliance with statutory notices. AREA. The City of London Registration District comprises an area of 672.7 acres (excluding tidal water and foreshore), or 1.051 square miles, equal to a square of 1.0252 miles to a side. The area of "Registration London" is 74,817 acres (excluding tidal water and foreshore), or 116.9 square miles, equal to a square of 10.81 miles to a side. DENSITY (1907). The actual number of people visiting the City daily is probably nearly 1,500,000. 12 ANNUAL RATEABLE VALUE. The rateable value of property within the administrative county of London was £43,897,571 on 6th April, 1907—whilst on the same date the corresponding rateable value of property in the City of London, including Temples, was £5,373,276, or nearly one-eighth of the whole County. This consists of two sub-districts, together containing a population of at the middle of 1907, calculated on the last Census Returns:— *St. Botolph 6,700 †St. Sepulchre and Allhallows 13,610 Total 20,310 The Night population, therefore, of the City of London Registration District at the middle of 1907 is taken at 20,310, and it is upon this figure that all vital statistics in this Report have been calculated. The Day population at the middle of 1907 was estimated at 380,150 ‡ persons. A Day Census taken in May, 1891, proved that 1,186,000 persons and 92,000 vehicles entered and left the City on the day the counting was made. In 1903 it was ascertained that in the week ending 19th July no less than 347,463 vehicles passed to and from the four City bridges, viz.:—London, Southwark, Blackfriars and Tower, equivalent to upwards of 18,000000 vehicles per annum. BIRTHS. During the year 1907, 268 births were registered in the City. The annual birth rate was at the rate of 13.1 per 1,000 persons of all ages, compared with a quinquennial mean rate of 14.1 per 1,000. The birth rates last year in the Sub-Districts were— St. Botolph 18.8 per 1,000. St. Sepulchre and Allhallows 10.4 „ In the Metropolis the birth rate in 1907 was 25.6 per 1,000, being the lowest hitherto recorded therein. This decline in the birth rate has been in operation in this country since 1876, and is common to nearly all European countries. * At the date of the last Census, this District included the "civil parish" of Glasshouse Yard. The parish was transferred to the Holborn Union by the Local Government Board Order, No. 43156, which came into operation on 29th September, 1901, and to the Registration District of Holborn (Finsbury Sub-District) on 1st January, 1902, making the Registration District of London City co-extensive with the Municipal City of London. All the Registration Districts in London are co-extensive with the Poor-Law Parishes or Unions except the District of Fulham. † On the 1st April, 1901. the Middle Temple was transferred from the Strand Sub-District to St. Sepulchre Sub-District. ‡ This figure is based upon the assumption that the number of persons residing, occupied or employed within the City during the active hours of the day has increased at the same rate since the last Day Census in 1891 that it did between that and the previous Day Census in 1881. 14 VACCINATION. Of the children born in 1907, 168 had been successfully vaccinated by the end of the year, 31 had died before the age for vaccination, and 3 were insusceptible. Vaccination was postponed by medical certificate in 13 cases, 34 had removed into other districts, and 13 vaccinations were pending at the end of the year. There were 7 cases of conscientious objection. The Public Vaccinators performed 1,385 re-vaccinations during the year. Vaccination Statistics in the City of London. Year. Number of Births. This measure received the Royal Assent on the 28th August, 1907, and came into operation on the 1st January following. It was introduced in the Commons by the President of the Local Government Board as a Government Bill. Its object is to relieve the "Conscientious Objector" of the necessity of in person satisfying a magistrate that he conscientiously believes that vaccination would be prejudicial to the health of the child before he can obtain an exemption certificate. By this Act the anti-vaccinationist can secure exemption from the operations of the Vaccination Act by making a Statutory declaration before a Commissioner for Oaths or Justice of the Peace, or other Officer authorised to receive a Statutory declaration that he has conscientious objection, and it remains to be seen whether this Act will cause any increase in the numbers of those obtaining exemption. The form of declaration required by the Act is as under :— SCHEDULE. Form of Declaration. I, A. B., of in the parish of in the County of being the parent (or person having 15 the custody) of a child named C. D., who was born on the day of ,19 , do hereby solemnly and sincerely declare that I conscientiously believe that vaccination would be prejudicial to the health of the child, and I make this solemn declaration conscientiously believing the same to be true, and by virtue of the provisions of the Statutory Declaration Act, 1853. Dated this day of , 19 Signed A. B. Declared before me, at on the day of E. F. A Commissioner for Oaths (or Justice of the Peace, or other Officer authorised to receive a Statutory declaration). On the 21st September last, the President of the Local Government Board issued a Vaccination Order to Boards of Guardians of every Poor Law Union in England and Wales, requiring Registrars of Births to give printed notice to parents of all children born after the 31st day of August, 1907, acquainting them of the new means of obtaining exemption, and to furnish them with a copy of the required form of declaration. Common sense is wholly opposed to any relaxation of the law requiring that all persons should be protected by vaccination, and the passing of this Act is a retrograde step, much to be deplored and at variance with the teaching of science. It would have been far better for the legislation to have made a forward movement and carried a Bill making re-vaccination compulsory, as obtains in Germany. MORTALITY. During the year under review, 853 deaths were registered in the District, including *692 persons dying in the City, but not belonging thereto. See Table, p. 20, for details. Deducting the latter 161 deaths remain, to which must be added 97 persons who died at City Institutions in outside districts, where they were registered. The mortality, therefore, from which the City death rate is calculated is 258. The death rate for 1907 was 12.7 per 1,000 per annum of the population (the previous year being 13.0), whilst that for the whole of the Metropolis was 14.6. These figures are not corrected for sex and age distribution. These deaths occurred in St. Bartholomew's Hospital. 16 Death Rates, per 1,000 per Annum. Year. The death rate from zymotic disease per 1,000 of the population was 0.44 compared with 1.41 in the Metropolis. Infantile Mortality. The deaths of children under one year of age to 1,000 births were 89, against 116 for the whole of London. The Table of infantile mortality on p. 17 shows the deaths of children under one year arranged according to monthly age periods. From this it will be seen that no less than 29 per cent. of these deaths occurred in the first month, 58 per cent. within the first three months, and 79 per cent. during the first six months of existence. Deaths in City Institutions. During 1907, 97 persons died in City Workhouses, Workhouse Infirmaries, or Lunatic Asylums, or no less than 37.6 per cent, of the whole City mortality, the corresponding figure for the Metropolis (1906) being 22.3 per cent. The Shelter in Golden Lane, Barbican, provided in accordance with Sec. 60 (4) of the Public Health (London) Act, 1891, which enacts that "sanitary "authorities shall provide free of charge temporary shelter or house "accommodation with necessary attendants for the members of any family 23 "in which any dangerous infectious disease has appeared, for the purpose of "enabling such dwellings to be disinfected by the sanitary authority," has been visited by eight families during the year. One family came from a home consisting of four rooms, five of three rooms, one of two rooms, and one of one room. 24 CONTAMINATED MILK. In investigating an outbreak of Enteric Fever at some premises in the City in April, 1907, it was found advisable to examine the milk supply. . ^ The establishment, which was a large one, was served with milk from three separate sources, and samples were therefore taken from each and submitted to Dr. Klein, who reported on them as under:— "Re Samples of Milk Nos. 1, 2 and 3, received on April 18th. "22nd April, 1907. "The analyses were made by taking the whole of the sediment of each sample "and inoculating with it the surface of two large Drigalski plates for each sample. "Although abundance of colonies of B. coli and of streptococci developed in these plates, there were no colonies that resembled either those of B. typhosus or "of B. Gaertner. "(Signed) E. KLEIN." Although these samples showed the absence of the specific germ of Enteric Fever, they indicated sewage pollution, and the dairymen were therefore asked to furnish the names of the farms supplying them. This information was at once given in two cases, but in the third the Dairy Company stated that it was not possible to do so after the lapse of time. A further sample was therefore taken, a duplicate being handed to the Company's representative at the time. Dr. Klein's report on this sample was as follows:— " Re Sample No. 4 of Milk received May 23rd. "30th May, 1907. "With the sediment of 300 c.c. made cultures for the detection of B. typhosus. "(a) Drigalski plates. " (b) Loffler's Malachite green Agar plates. "In both these sets colonies appeared, which by their colour and appearance "bore a resemblance to B. typhosus. "From the Malachite green Agar plate a number of promising colonies were "subcultured. "Some of these gave striking agglutination with typhoid serum in dilation of "1.50, but the character of the subcultures clearly proved that none of them are "B. typhosus; their behaviour in shake gelatine (gas bubbles), in Neutral red broth "(greenish fluorescent), in Lactose litmus peptone (red and gas), in Drigalski plate "(red colonies)—clearly showed that we were dealing with some coli-like microbes. "The result, then, is—that from the sediment of 300 c.c. of the above milk no "B. typhosus was isolated. "(Signed) E. KLEIN." 25 This also proving unsatisfactory, all the Dealers were asked to produce:— 1. A Report from a Veterinary Surgeon as to the health of the cows. 2. A Report from the Medical Officer of Health of the District as to the sanitary condition of the premises. These particulars were obtained in each instance. Summarised they were as under:— No. 1. (Stafford). Premises.—"In fair condition, some small repairs are required to "ceiling and wall. Water is being analysed." Cows.—"Of 19, 17 are in sound health, one has lung disease "permanent following pneumonia, possibly Tubercular, and another "emaciated udder doubtful thickened." In both cases sale is recommended, but no mention is made as to the necessity for taking precautions to prevent the animals being used for milk or food elsewhere. No. 2. (Suffolk). Cows.—"17 cows in healthy condition." Premises.—"Sanitary condition defective." No. 3. (Suffolk). Premises.—"The cows as a rule are not attended to before being —"milked, which is done in a portion of a large covered building, the "cows standing on dry straw, and earth underneath which prevents "any washing down. The building to which the milk is taken for "cooling is very small, and somewhat dilapidated. The water is "of good quality." Cows.—"There are 25 cows, and of this number 23 are in "apparently sound, healthy and good condition, the other two have "indurated udders." With reference to the samples from Suffolk it was ascertained that the Local Authority had not made any Regulations under the Dairies, Cowsheds and Milkshops Order of 1885. In each case the diseased cows were removed and the sanitary defects remedied. These facts furnish an illustration of the laxity that prevails amongst farmers and milk dealers with regard to their responsibilities to the public in securing the purity of such an important article of food as milk. Nor are either these parties likely to take any steps in the right direction unless they 26 are compelled to do so. Unfortunately, in the present state of the law there are great difficulties placed in the way, not the least of these being the apathy of Local Authorities with respect to carrying out their obligations under the Dairies, Cowsheds and Milkshops Order of 1885. With regard to these, a Return presented to the House of Commons on 13th May, 1907, shows— (a) The names of the Councils of Boroughs and Urban and Rural Districts who have made Regulations under the Dairies, Cowsheds and Milkshops Order of 1885, and (b) The number of Councils of each class who have not made such Regulations. From this Return we learn that while "The Corporation of the City of "London have made Regulations,* in the rest of the Metropolis Regulations "are in force which were made by the Metropolitan Board of Works."† The number of Authorities outside the Metropolis that have made, and have not made such Regulations are as under:— No. that have made Regulations. No. that have not made Regulations. Total. Councils of Boroughs 290 36 326 Councils of Urban Districts other than Boroughs 684 126 810 Councils of Rural Districts 493 165 658 1,467 327 1,794 In the aggregate, 18.2 per cent. have not made Regulations. Taken separately the percentage of these are as follows:— Councils of Boroughs 11.0 per cent. Councils of Urban Districts other than Boroughs 15.5 „ Councils of Rural Districts 25.1 „ It will be seen that approximately in one-fourth of the Rural Districts the Councils have failed to take advantage of the opportunity the law affords of exercising proper control over the source of milk supply in the districts over which they are supposed to exercise sanitary control. The Parliamentary Return from which the above figures are taken is somewhat incomplete, as while it gives the names and numbers of those Authorities "regulating" Dairies, Cowsheds and Milkshops, it gives the numbers only of those that have failed to do so. * These were last revised in 1903. † The Metropolitan Board of Works were succeeded by the London County Council in 1889. 27 The making of such Regulations is one thing, but the strict enforcement of them when made is the essential point. The condition of many of the milks that arrive at City Railway termini indicates that there is much room for improvement in that direction. In dealing with this question two points are clear— Firstly.—The urgent need for general legislation with regard to the milk supply. This must deal with the question at the farm itself, and must be the duty of the Local Authority. No half-hearted measure giving the Metropolis, or any other district, power to exclude infected or dirty milk is of any real service. If milk from a particular source is excluded by order from one place, it will be sent to another, either to be consumed therein, or possibly to be again forwarded to the excluded area. Secondly.—The Local Authority where the farm is situated alone can effectively deal with the question. If there is a difficulty in getting them to act in some cases by reason of vested interests or other causes, there should be no difficulty in devising a system whereby the Board of Agriculture and Fisheries would act in case of default. The enormous cost of an Authority, such as the London County Council or the Corporation, sending its Medical Officer and Veterinary Surgeon all over the country should alone be sufficient to condemn the system. But there are still graver objections. An outside Authority would act at a great disadvantage, and would meet with much opposition in carrying out its investigations, while the fact that the work was being done by a Central Authority would undoubtedly tend to diminish the feeling of responsibility of the Local Authority, who would not unnaturally be loth to spend time, money and energy on doing work which, if left undone, would be taken up by others. Any steps taken should be in the direction of increasing the responsibility of Local Authorities, and of quickening them to a lively sense of the same. The case of default can easily be dealt with, without in any way imperilling the co-operation of these Authorities, who are ready and willing to do their duty. In view of the great importance of this question, I would suggest that the Government be urged forthwith to promote general legislation with regard to milk supply on the lines indicated in this and previous Reports. 28 The following extract from the "Times" newspaper of the 12th February last indicates the attitude of the Government towards the question of milk supply* :— House of Lords.—Tuesday, llth February, 1908. "MILK SUPPLY. "The Earl of Northbrook asked the President of the Board of Agriculture "whether it was the intention of the Government to introduce legislation with "regard to the inilk supply during the present Session ; and, in the event of such "legislation being introduced by the Local Government Board, what steps the "Board of Agriculture proposed to take for the purpose of safeguarding the "interests of agriculture. He said that the dairy farmers realized that legislation "was not only desirable, but was necessary to ensure the supply of pure and "wholesome milk to the consumers. They would welcome such legislation if it were "just and equitable, and were applied to the whole country. The present system of "controlling the milk supply was eminently unsatisfactory. It was based on orders "which were permissive, and legislation which had been piecemeal. Some local "authorities had made rules under the Dairies and Milkshops Orders of 1885-86 "which were carried out in a satisfactory manner ; others had made orders which "were carried out in a perfunctory way ; in a large number of cases rural and "urban sanitary authorities had not chosen to make any regulations at all, and in "their districts the milk supply was without any supervision whatever. The "London County Council had powers of dealing with the milk supply under "three Acts, and were endeavouring to get more in a General Powers Bill "this Session. In a considerable number of districts there were two, three, "or four authorities with overlapping jurisdiction, with the result that "unnecessary burdens were put upon the farmers. A general public Bill was, in "these circumstances, desirable in the interests of both producer and consumer. It "had been announced by the President of the Local Government Board that the "Government thought it desirable that there should be legislation during the present "Session. His question invited a more definite statement. In speeches both by "the President of the Board of Agriculture and by Sir E. Strachey, who spoke for "the Board in the other House, farmers had received warnings as to possible "legislation, which had produced some feeling of insecurity and alarm, which, he "hoped, would now be allayed. He acknowledged the scientific equipment of the "Local Government Board, but reminded the President of the Board of Agriculture "that the milk supply required to be dealt with from a practical as well as a ''theoretical point of view, and that any question concerned with cattle should be "dealt with by the competent advisers of the Board of Agriculture, in whom the "agriculturists had confidence. He hoped the President of the Board of Agriculture "would give consideration to the suggestion that before any legislation was "introduced an inquiry, Parliamentary or departmental, should be held into the " question of the milk supply of the country. "Earl Carrington said he was glad to be able to announce that he had received a "letter from his right hon. friend the President of the Local Government Board stating "that it was the intention of the Government to introduce legislation this Session in "respect to the milk supply of the country. He agreed that the milk industry was "not in the satisfactory condition which every one desired ; and as the great object "of the present Government was practical legislation he felt sure that in the * See foot note on page 74. 29 "forthcoming Bill the matter would be dealt with practically. With regard to the "second part of the noble earl's question, he could not make a definite announcement "until the proposals of the Government were a little more developed ; but he could "promise that he would do everything in his power legitimately to protect the "farmers in the proposed legislation consistently with the interests of the public "welfare. As to the suggested public enquiry, he would consult with the President "of the Local Government Board. He might add, as the matter was somewhat "connected with the present subject, that the news from Scotland was that no fresh "outbreaks of foot-and-mouth disease had taken place, and that the disease was "being confined to a very limited area. "The Earl of Roskbery.—As regards that last point, has the noble Earl "received any representation from Linlithgow on the subject of the restrictions? "Earl Carrington.—I have. "The Earl of Roskbery. —Does my noble friend mean to pay any attention to " it? " Earl Cakrington.—I am afraid I must have notice of that question. "The Earl of Onslow did not think the reply of the President of the Board of "Agriculture carried the matter very far. The public were already aware that it "was the intention of the President of the Local Government Board to introduce a "Bill dealing with the question of the milk supply. He thought the Government might "be reminded that there was such a place as the House of Lords. That House was "not very busy just now; and there was no reason why the proposed Bill should "not be brought forward there, so that farmers might have an early opportunity of "expressing their opinions on it. "The Earl of Camperdown supported the suggestion that the Bill should be "first introduced in the House of Lords. At the present time there was no indication "that any business would be given them to do by the Government. "Lord Tweedmouth said he was not in a position to state whether the "Government were prepared at once to introduce the measure in that House. He "hoped, however, it would soon be introduced, whether in the Lords or in the "Commons. In the course of the next few weeks noble lords would have two "Scottish Bills to discuss. "The Earl of Rosebery.—After they have been discussed in the Commons." CARRIAGE OF MILK BY RAIL. In January, 1904, the Board of Agriculture and Fisheries issued a Leaflet containing correspondence, passed in 1899, between themselves, the Board of Trade, and the Railway Companies with regard to the conveyance of milk by rail in locked and sealed churns, which shows that all the principal railway 30 companies make no difference in their charges when sealed cans are used, which are stamped with their tare weight, and merely reserve the right to open locked cans when there is any reasonable doubt as to the accuracy of the consignment. I have repeatedly pointed out the undesirability of consigning milk to London railway stations in unsealed vessels. The Board of Agriculture and Fisheries last year took further steps for preventing pilfering or contamination of milk in transit by issuing on the 27th February a circular letter to makers in Great Britain of churns for carriage of milk by rail. The Board point out that two of the principal dairy farmers' associations have for several years recommended their members to send their milk by rail in sealed churns, and that the railway companies do not object to carry milk in sealed churns, provided the tare weight is stamped on the outside of the churn. The Board suggest that the following points should be kept in view in connection with the construction of milk churns :— (1) The churn should have the tare weight stamped on the outside. (2) The lid of the churn should be constructed so as to facilitate sealing. (3) The churn should be constructed so as to prevent, as far as possible, the removal of milk from the churn while sealed. (4) The churn should be constructed so as to prevent water being added to the milk in the churn while sealed. (5) The churn should be constructed so as to prevent dust or dirt from being blown or washed into the churn when the lid is affixed. There is no difficulty in making churns that fulfil these conditions, and more than one firm has placed such on the market. It is unfortunate that the Board have no power to enforce their recommendations, and failing authority for this purpose it is doubtful whether much immediate improvement will be effected even with the co-operation of the various trade societies and associations throughout the country. At present only a small proportion of milk sent to the metropolis is conveyed in sealed churns, but it is to be hoped that the action taken by the Board of Agriculture and Fisheries will induce manufacturers, when receiving orders for new churns, to advise the adoption of churns made in accordance with the recommendations of the Board. 31 SHELLFISH POLLUTION. In ray annual report for 1906 it was pointed out that Dr. Bulstrode's report to the Local Government Board in 189fi showed conclusively that at that date, in the greater part of the layings, shellfish were cultivated under conditions that render them specially liable to sewage pollution, and although since then some attempt has been made iD some places to modify the evil, much remains to be done. The Fourth Report of the Royal Commission on Sewage Disposal, dated 28th December, 1903, contains the following expression of opinion. "After carefully examining the whole of the evidence on this point (viz. : extent "of disease due to the consumption of contaminated shellfish) we are satisfied that "a considerable number of cases of enteric fever and other illness are caused by the "consumption of shellfish which have been exposed to sewage contamination. "There can, however, be no doubt that the evil is sufficiently grave to demand "a remedy." These observations indicate the gravity of the situation, and although they were made no less than four years ago, up to the present they do not seem to have made any more impression on the legislation than did Dr. Bulstrode's report seven years earlier. Meanwhile constant confirmation is received that the danger from eating contaminated shellfish is an ever present one, indeed, being far more real and in need of being dealt with from a public health point of view than many reforms receiving more attention from the public. As in former years requests were received to make investigations with regard to shellfish purchased in the City suspected of having caused illness. OYSTERS. In a case reported from Walthamstow, a resident in that district who was attacked with Enteric Fever—which terminated fatally—was taken ill soon after eating oysters purchased in the City. Samples of Oysters were purchased at the shop supplying them, and submitted to Dr. Klein, who reported upon them as follows:— " Re Sample of Oysters, received November 22nd. "Eight oysters were examined thus :—After thoroughly cleansing the outside, "the shell was opened, the liquor drained off, and the entire fish finely minced. "Eight fish yielded about 8-10 c.c. turbid thick fluid. This fluid was examined "for B. typhosus by means of large Drigalski plates—eight plates in all, each plate "receiving a little over one-tenth c.c. of the turbid thick fluid. "The result was this:—Amongst numerous colonies of B. coli communis, there "were present a considerable number of bluish colonies, some of these resembling in 32 "aspect and mode of growth colonies of B. typhosus. Examined under the micro"scope only four gave the primary tests for B. typhosus, viz.: being cylindrical "motile bacilli in size like those of B. typhosus. Analysed by sub-culture it was "found, however, that one was a liquifier, two others were B. fluorescens puttidus "(forming bluish pigment). The fourth failed to give any indication of clumping "with typhoid serum, and failed to grow in phenel-broth- "It is, therefore, clear that no B. typhosus was isolated from this sample "I think at the same time that the oysters were highly polluted, containing "abundance of B. col: communis, proteus vulgaris, and bacillus fluorescens "puttidus." A copy of this analysis was sent to the Fishmongers' Company and the salesman informed as to the condition of the oysters. In an interview with him some difficulty was found in ascertaining exactly the source from which they were derived, and further samples were taken and submitted to analysis with the following result :— " Re Samples of Oysters received November 28th. "These oysters were examined in exactly the same manner as the previous "sample, viz. :—By Drigalski plates. "The result of this examination showed the presence in all of the oysters of "abundance of B. coli communis. A large number of colonies simulating "B. typhosus were tested, and were found that none of them complied with the "character of B. typhosus. "Owing to the abundance of B. coli communis, I consider these oysters unclean." On the 3rd December, further samples were taken from so-called "Whitstable" and "Dutch" Oysters, the result of this analysis is as follows :— " Re Samples of Oysters, No. 3 and No. 4. "Received in the afternoon of December 3rd, by Inspector Hills, labelled :— "Sample No. 3—Whitstable. "Sample No. 4—Dutch. "The result of the bacterioscopic analysis of these samples is as follows :— "Whitstable Oysters No. 3. "On opening, two or three were found shrivelled up and discoloured, no liquor in "shell. "Of eight oysters, five contain abundance of B. coli communis. One further "oyster contains some other kind of B. coli. All of them contain the spores of B. "enteritidis sporogenes. A Drigalski plate made with one-fifth c.c. of shell-water "contains great crowds of microbic colonies, capable of growing at 37° C., amongst "them some colonies of Porteus vulgaris. "Dutch Oysters No. 4. "Of eight oysters, six have abundance of B. coli communis, one further oyster "has some kind of B. coli. Half of them have spores of B. enteritidis sporogenes. "A Drigalski plate made with one-fifth c.c. of shell-water contains very numerous "colonies, capable of growing at 37° C. 33 "When the same procedure of making Drigalski plates of clean oysters is used, "there appear on such a Drigalski plate kept at 37° C. either lio colonies at all, or "very few only, thus showing that a clean oyster and its shell-water are practically "free of microbes capable of growing at 37° C., whereas in the above two cases "there were contained in the shell-water crowds of such microbes, and this in "agreement with the results of the other analysis above stated, viz., that both "samples contain abundance of sewage or filth microbes, viz., B. coli communis and "spores of B. enteritidis sporogenes. "For these reasons, 1 consider both samples unclean and polluted with sewage "or filth. (Signed) "E. KLEIN." Another fatal case of Enteric, which the deceased's friends attributed to oysters eaten in the City, was reported from Exeter, but on examination by Dr. Klein of a sample purchased at the shop where they were bought showed these to be quite clean. Both the Fishmongers' Company and the Vendor of the contaminated oysters were acquainted with the results, and the latter at once gave an undertaking to discontinue the sale of all oysters except those derived from sources known to be free from pollution. In the course of this enquiry it was ascertained by the officers of the Fishmongers' Company that the oysters in question had come from some layings that were above suspicion, but had subsequently been stored in some sewage-polluted pits. The Company immediately took action, which resulted in the absolute closure of these pits by the owners. A case of considerable importance to the public was heard at the Lexden and Winstree Sessions on the 21st August last, when an oyster merchant of Wyvenhoe was summoned at the instance of the Wyvenhoe Urban District Council for selling oysters that were unfit for human food. The summons was taken out under Section 107 of the Public Health Act, 1875, and Section 28 of the Public Health Act, 1890. The oysters in question, which were Portuguese relaid, had been sold to an officer of the Fishmongers' Company, who submitted them to Dr. Klein for analysis, who reported on them as follows :— " Analysis of Oysters. "Of six Portuguese oysters—small six have abundance of B. coli communis. "Two specially examined—both have enteritidis spores. "Of six Portuguese oysters, large—all have abundance of B. coli communis. "Two specially examined—both have enteritidis spores. "Result—oysters distinctly sewage polluted, therefore unfit for food in their raw "E. KLEIN." 34 It was proved that defendant had been previously warned by the Fishmongers' Company that some o£ his pits were contaminated. The defendant was fined £20 and costs, including £5 for expenses of analysis. As an illustration of the character of the local surroundings I quote the following description from an article discussing the prosecution in the " Fish Trades Gazette" :— "The town of Wyvenhoe has a population of 2,700, and its sewers "empty themselves at various points on the foreshore from the ship"building yard on the West, to the gasworks on the East, creating "what is nothing else than a filthy public nuisance. On the opposite "side of the river higher up is Rowhedge, with sewers on its foreshore. "On Wyvenhoe foreshore defendant has a yard adjoining the pit "compulsorily closed four years ago, and on either side of the yard are "two sewers not many feet apart, one of which is connected with a "slaughter house. As the mouths of the sewers are high up on the "foreshore, their contents at low water gullies and pools. Such we "are told is the unenviable situation of this place ! " Upon the facts becoming known, the Fishmongers' Company were communicated with, and it was ascertained that steps had been taken to prevent any shellfish being sent to the City that had been taken from the place where the condemned oysters were stored. Such a state of things as that described above should be an impossibility, and it reflects little credit upon our Governing Bodies, that although the need for legislation with regard to shellfish became so pressing so long ago as 1898, that a deputation from the authorities of twenty-seven large towns, to the President of the Local Government Board, urged the necessity of legislation in order to minimise the dangers to which the public are exposed through the consumption of sewage contaminated oysters, and other shellfish, without however committing itself to the particular lines which such legislation should follow—yet the only evidence of any movement in the required direction is the Report of the Royal Commission on Sewage Disposal previously referred to. As the present office holders are pledged to effect improvements in the domestic welfare and governance of the people, the present may be an opportune time to again urge upon the Local Government Board the importance of dealing with this question as speedily as possible. The longer the matter is delayed, not only does the danger become greater, but also more difficult to deal with. 35 WINKLES. Four cases were reported during the year, in which winkles were suspected of having caused Enteric Fever, the winkles having been obtained from Billingsgate in each instance. The first case occurred at Wandsworth in February, another at Woolwich in March, and a third at East Ham in May, but in neither instance could any definite information be obtained as to the source of supply. The fourth case of this kind occurred in September at Wandsworth, but as a month had elapsed since the winkles were purchased, the source of supply, as in the previous case, could not be identified. It was ascertained, however, that they were obtained through an agent in Glasgow, who purchased supplies from a great number of places in the Western Isles of Scotland. Some of these are known to be polluted, and the authorities in which they are situated have taken steps to prevent winkles from being collected from them. 36 ROYAL COMMISSION ON TUBERCULOSIS- The second Interim Report of the Royal Commission, appointed on the 31st August, 1901, to enquire into the relations of Human and Animal Tuberculosis, was issued in January, 1907. The reference to this Commission was to enquire and report with respect to tuberculosis. (1.) Whether the disease in animals and man is one and the same. (2.) Whether animals and man can be reciprocally infected with it. (3.) Under what conditions, if at all, the transmission of disease from animals to man takes place, and what are the circumstances, favourable or unfavourable, to such transmission. It will be remembered that this enquiry was instituted in consequence of a pronouncement by Dr. Koch at the British Congress on Tuberculosis in 1901, when he expressed the following opinion :— "I feel justified in maintaining that human tuberculosis differs from "bovine, and cannot be transmitted to cattle. "The infection of human beings (from animals) is but a very rare "occurrence. 1 should estimate the extent of infection by the milk and "flesh of tubercular cattle, and the butter made of their milk, as hardly "greater than that of hereditary transmission, and I therefore do not "deem it advisable to take any measures against it." These views being in opposition to those held by the majority of experts who had studied the subject, and as, if accepted, they would have involved the abandonment of all inspection and destruction of meat affected with tuberculosis, it became imperative that the question should be the subject of an immediate and exhaustive investigation. In May, 1904, the Commission were in a position to prove that human tuberculosis could be transmitted to cattle, and on the 13th May of that year issued a first interim report, which stated :— "We have thought it our duty to make this short interim report for "the reason that the result at which we have arrived, namely, that "tubercle of human origin can give rise in the bovine animal to "tuberculosis identical with ordinary bovine tuberculosis, seems to us to "show quite clearly that it would be most unwise to frame or modify "legislative measures in accordance with the view that human and "bovine tubercle bacilli are specifically different from each other, and "that the disease caused bv one is a wholly different thing from the "disease caused by the other." 37 In the second Interim Report, dated January, 1907, it was shown that human beings could be, and frequently were, infected with tuberculosis through the consumption of milk. The Commission reported thereon in the following terms :— "There can be no doubt that in a certain number of cases the "tuberculosis occurring in the human subject, especially in children, is "the direct result of the introduction into the human body of the bacillus "of bovine tuberculosis, and there can also be no doubt that in the "majority, at least, of these cases the bacillus is introduced through "cow's milk. Cow's milk containing bovine tubercle is clearly a cause "of tuberculosis, and of latal tuberculosis in man." "A very considerable amount of disease and loss of life, especially "among the young, must be attributed to the use of cow's milk contain"ing tubercle bacilli. The presence of tubercle bacilli in cow's milk can "be detected, though with some difficulty, if the proper means be "adopted, and such milk ought never to be used for food. There is far "less difficulty in recognising clinically that a cow is distinctly suffering "from tuberculosis, in which case she may be yielding tuberculous milk. "The milk coming from such a cow ought not to form part of human "food, and, indeed, ought not to be used as food at all. "Our results clearly point to the necessity of measures more stringent "than those at present enforced being taken to prevent the sale or the "consumption of such milk." Since these reports appeared, three large volumes have been published giving a complete history of all the experiments that led the Commission to make the pronouncements referred to above. The books contain an enormous mass of material, including literary matter, illustrations and reports on inoculation, feeding and other experiments, that although exceedingly interesting and valuable from a scientific point of view, are of no attraction to the general public. One of the most interesting features of the appendices is contained in the summary of feeding experiments. These were made with bovine tubercle bacillus :— 1. To ascertain the relative susceptibility of different species of animals to this mode of inoculation. 2. To study the anatomical distribution of tuberculosis produced by feeding. Many species of animals were employed for this purpose. 38 In all these it was found that wherever generalised tuberculosis is produced by feeding, the lungs are generally affected often to a greater extent than any other organ in the body. These experiments show that tuberculosis is readily set up in pigs, and that in young ones it is usually of a progressive type. The adult pig, although readily affected, is much more resistant than the young one. The young pig was found to be undoubtedly the most susceptible to tuberculosis of all the animal species used in this series of experiments. In this connection I may mention that the leaflet No. 100, published by the Board of Agriculture and Fisheries for the guidance of pig breeders in Januar}r, 1904, was revised in May of this year, and it is pointed out in the later edition that tuberculosis in pigs is probably caused chiefly by feeding them on refuse milk from dairies in which cows with tubercular udders exist, and on the offal of diseased cattle. Inoculation experiments were also carried out on a large scale, and it is interesting to note that they showed that the chimpanzee and the various species of monkeys used are highly susceptible to the bovine tubercular bacillus, this high susceptibility being so clearly established that it seemed to be unnecessary to perform any more experiments on the same lines. As these animals are the most nearly related to the human race, it is not unlikely that tuberculosis can be conveyed to man in the same manner. Butchers, slaughtermen and others engaged in the meat trade should exercise the greatest care in the pursuit of their calling, and when slaughtering or cutting up any animal that shows the slightest suspicion of disease, they should thoroughly cleanse the implements used before they are again employed. The appearance of these volumes are very welcome, as they indicate clearly that the Local Government Board had the strongest warrant for the recommendation they issued in their circular letter dated 11th March, 1899, for the guidance of sanitary authorities and their officers in any action they may have to take with regard to tuberculous meat. Further, they emphasise the pressing necessity for immediate legislation with regard to the control of both meat and milk supplies throughout the country, there being almost daily evidence of the present unsatisfactory state of affairs. SPITTING IN RAILWAY CARRIAGES. The exhibition of cautionary notices against the evil practice of expectoration in railway carriages has not been without some good effect, but at the same time there is much room for improvement in manners of a certain section of the public who disregard them. 39 The present type of notice adopted is usually in the form of a request to passengers to abstain from this objectionable habit, 011 the grounds of cleanliness and danger to the public health, but it is advisable that it should be more generally known that the railway companies are armed with powers of repression of this nuisance under one of their bye-laws, which is to the following effect:— "No person shall spit upon the floor or upon any part of any carriage "using or being on the railway, or upon the platform at any station "of the Company, or upon the floor, side or wall of any hall, office, "waiting-room, refreshment-room, public room, or public passage at any "station of the Company. Any person infringing or not observing this "bye-law and regulation shall be liable to a penalty, and in case of non"compliance with the request of any servant or agent of the Company "to desist, may, without prejudice to any such penalty, be forthwith "removed from the Company's premises by or under the direction of any "such servant or agent." The penalty referred to is a fine not exceeding forty shillings for the first offence, and not exceeding Mo for any subsequent offence. It will be noticed that under this bye-law, not only is it a punishable offence to spit upon the floor or upon any part of any carriage using or being on the railway, but any person who does not comply with the request of any servant or agent of the Company, may be forthwith removed from the carriage. Your Medical Officer suggests, therefore, that the several railway Companies who have adopted this bye-law, and have stations in the City, be asked to direct attention to it by public notice posted on their premises, or that if more agreeable to any Company, the Corporation might offer to provide such notices, if their exhibition be permitted. CEREBRO SPINAL FEVER. The spread of this disease in Central Europe and America having given rise in some quarters to a doubt whether it may not have newly extended to, or been developing in this country, the Local Government Board in August, 1905, addressed a Memorandum to all Sanitary Authorities dealing generally with the characteristic symptoms of the disease, and especially with the minor anomalous manifestations. On the recommendation of your Medical Officer, a copy of this Memorandum was sent to every medical practitioner in the City. This is reproduced on 40 p 42 of the Annual Report for 1905, and on p. 36 of the Report for 1906 will be found a brief account of a fatal case that occurred in the City in that year. On the 21st February, 1907, a circular letter was received from the Local Government Board pointing out that Cerebro-Spinal Fever had recently been somewhat prevalent in parts of Scotland and Ireland, and that a few cases had occurred in this country. Sanitary Authorities were therefore advised to be on the alert to detect the presence of the disease in their district, or to satisfy themselves as to its absence. The Board also directed attention to their Memorandum issued in August, 1905, referred to above, and copies of the same were again issued by the Corporation. On the 26th February, 1907, the London County Council made an order requiring, for the period of six months, the notification of cases of Cerebrospinal Fever occurring within the administrative County of London. This order was approved by the Local Government Board, and came into operation on the 12th March. The Council made another order in August, extending the notification of this disease for a further period of eighteen months as from and including the 13th September. This was also approved by the Local Government Board, and accordingly came into operation on that date. No case has been reported in the City since the disease became notifiable. DIPHTHERIA IN PUBLIC ELEMENTARY SCHOOLS. In January, 1907, your Medical Officer reported that the Medical Officer for the Education Department of the London County Council had informed him that the Council had passed the following Resolution : "That the Education Committee be authorised to refuse, during the "presence of diphtheria in any district, re-admission to school of children "excluded on account of diphtheria or sore throat, until such children "shall have obtained a medical certificate of freedom from infection "based on a bacteriological examination." The letter suggested that in order to give effect to this Resolution, it was proposed that a card should be used for such cases, giving the reason for exclusion, and stating that the necessary certificate could be signed by their own Doctor or the Medical Officer of Health. 41 While in accord with the view expressed in the Council's Resolution, with regard to a bacteriological examination in the circumstances as stated, the Sanitary Committee accepted your Medical Officer's recommendation, that in cases where the parents were unable to obtain the necessary certificates from their own Doctor, they should be directed to apply to the Medical Officer of the Education Department. The Committee's view of the matter having been communicated to the London County Council, the latter undertook that their Medical Officer would furnish bacteriological reports on specimens submitted to them when necessary. CLEANSING AND DISINFECTION OF SCHOOLS. With the object of preventing the occurrence of cases of infectious diseases of all kinds among school children, it would be well if all class-rooms, and school books were periodically disinfected as a matter of routine. A case is reported from America when, after the disinfection of upwards of 100,000 books used in the Public Schools of Buffalo, it was noticed that there had been a decrease in contagious diseases, particularly Scarlet Fever and Measles. With regard to the disinfection of school-rooms, the Committee on Disinfection of the American Health Association, in the review of the work of the year 1904, state that remarkable results have followed the occasional use of a suitable disinfectant on the floors of school-houses, and that infectious cold and other dust-borne diseases were thereby much lessened among the scholars. The Scotch Education Department issued a Memorandum in November, 1907, giving full instructions as to the most efficient way of cleansing and disinfecting schools, and articles used in the course of instruction. The directions given, were prepared by the expert advisers of the Local Government Board for Scotland. They are based upon actual experience, and take full account of practical difficulties. Great stress is laid upon the importance of periodical cleansing, and disinfection of schoolrooms, and to the dangers arising from dusty atmosphere, dust being one of the factors in the dissimination of infectious disease. It is for this reason therefore, that periodic disinfection is advisable, even when no known infectious disease has been present. The memorandum deals fully with the following points :— i. The sources of dirt in schools, and the danger attending it. ii. The general principles of cleansing and disinfection wherein moist cleansing of floors, walls &c., is strongly advocated. Full specific directions are also given how best to disinfect schoolrooms and school appliances. 42 The Corporation have no power to enforce disinfection except in cases where infectious disease has occurred, and the Education Authorities should be therefore invited to give careful consideration to the suggestions of the Scotch Education Department, with a view to their adoption in all schools under their charge. CLEANSING VERMINOUS CHILDREN ATTENDING PUBLIC ELEMENTARY SCHOOLS. At the latter end of 1906, your Medical Officer reported that some suggestion had been received from the Education Department of the London County Council, with a view of checking the number of children attending the schools in a verminous condition. Two alternative schemes were proposed, one where the name and address of any child in a verminous condition is forwarded to the Medical Officer of Health, whose Inspector calls at the House and leaves an order entitling the child to a bath and disinfection at the public expense, the other was for the Medical Officer of Health to furnish the Medical Officer of the Education Department of the London County Council with a supply of cards, also entitling the child to a free bath and disinfection. At your Medical Officer's recommendation, the Committee decided to adopt the first suggestion, because it would involve the inspection of the child's home when he made his call, whereas in the second, he might remain ignorant of the number of infected children or the places where they resided, and it is most essential that houses of verminous children should be strictly looked after. Arrangements were therefore made in February, 1907, that the names and addresses of verminous children should be sent to your Medical Officer, and if after the inspection it was necessary, an order on the Guardians for cleansing and disinfecting the child would be granted. Such orders will only be issued with respect to children residing within the City. 43 CREMATION. The number of Cremations carried out at the Corporation Crematorium at Uford last year was 18, being 5 less than in 1906, and a reference to the Table on page 48, shows that a slight decrease is recorded at several other Crematoria, the total number in the Kingdom during 1907 being 706, as compared with 743 in 1906. In view of the steady annual increase in the numbers from 1885 to 1906, it would appear that this is merely a casual fluctuation, and does not indicate in any way that the process is becoming less popular. Apart from any matter of sentiment, there can be no question that the best, quickest, and most sanitary method of disposing of a dead body is to reduce it to ashes in a properly constructed furnace. Cremation solves the question of intra mural burial. It would be far more convenient and more in accordance with modern science if the remains of those the Country honoured by laying them to rest in our Abbeys and Cathedrals, were always cremated, and their ashes alone deposited in the place of sepulchre. Moreover, our churches could receive within their walls, without danger to the living, as in the past, the remains of those who had been their supporters during their lifetime, should their survivors desire it. The managers of several of the Crematoria in the United Kingdom have favoured me with some notes on their experiences of Cremation. The Hon. Secretary of the Birmingham Crematorium remarks :— "I have found that in many cases where a deceased person had left "instructions for cremation, the relatives being adverse to cremation, "would not permit of the body being cremated, and I have also found "that undertakers can greatly influence relatives of a deceased person "either for or against cremation in accordance with their own ideas. "We have recently learned of cases in which the above facts have taken "place." The Superintendent of the Sheffield Crematorium states :— "Although the total shows an increase of 12 only over last year, I am "of opinion that Cremation is becoming more popular." 44 The Superintendent of the Corporation Crematorium at Bradford, referring to the year's work, remarks :— "These figures are not large, and we cannot say that we have made "much progress during the year. I believe, however, that cremation is "becoming more widely known and better understood. Still the people "are not sufficiently educated on the subject." Referring to the decrease in the number of Cremations carried out during the year by the London Cremation Company, both at Woking and Golder's Green, the Secretary writes :— "The growing interest in the subject of Cremation is evidenced by "the increased number of enquiries and the large number of visitors who "avail themselves of the facilities offered for inspecting the buildings "and apparatus when a cremation is not proceeding, gives us reason to "think that the set back is quite a temporary one, and not to be regarded as a decline in the movement." The Secretary of the Scottish Burial Reform and Cremation Society, Ltd., Glasgow, remarks :— "We are finding as the years go past that Cremation as a means of "disposing of the dead is gradually gaining in public favour." The Manager of the Leeds Crematorium notes that:— "The progress seems to be slow, and the prejudice against Cremation "stubborn." The suggestion has been made that the Home Office Regulations should no longer require : (1) the applicant to make a Statutory Declaration ; and (2) that the second certificate should not be confined to a limited body of medical men, but that any medical practitioner who has been on the Register for five years, should, without further qualification, be permitted to sign the second certificate. It has been said that the retention of these regulations tends in a great measure to retard the spread of Cremation, but I am quite unable to endorse this opinion. Opponents of Cremation would be only too ready to make adverse use of any attempts to relax the present regulations which, very properly, have been made exceedingly stringent, and it would certainly be most unwise to lend any countenance to the suggested alteration. It will be seen from the foregoing that apart from the slight set-back of last year, the practice is steadily increasing in favour in this country. In other countries the movement is making steady advancement, as will be seen from the following figures.* * The figures, &c., in the following notes are taken from the German publication "Die Flamme," kindly placed at my disposal by the Secretary of the Cremation Society of England. 45 Germany. The first Crematorium was opened at Gotha in December, 1878 ; a second was established at Heidelberg in 1891 ; a third at Hamburg in 1892 ; and a fourth at Jena in 1898, Other towns followed suit, and'.there are now fourteen towns with Crematoria. The total number of bodies incinerated in Germany from 1878 to the middle of last year was 13,614. Denmark. The only Crematorium in this Country was established at Copenhagen in 1893, where 508 cremations have taken place up to the middle of last year. Italy. There are now no less than 28 Crematoria in Italy, the first being erected at Mailand in 1876 ; a second was built at Cremona in 1881, and every year other towns established similar appliances. From 1876 until the middle of last year 6,513 bodies have been cremated in this country. France. A Crematorium was established at Pere-la-Chaise, in Paris, 1889, and from that date to 1906 inclusive, 4,204 cremations for private persons took place. During the same period 82,758 hospital remains and unclaimed bodies, &c., were also dealt with. Provincial Crematoria have also been established at Rouen, Lyons, Rheims and Marseilles, but no figures are available. I am informed that the three latter are used mainly as adjuncts to the local cemetery, and that when it is full, the bodies of those who have, by agreement, been interred for a term of years, are exhumed, and their remains cremated. Russia. Crematoria for St. Petersburg.—The President of the Sanitary Committee has submitted his report to the St. Petersburg Town Council in favour of the establishment of a crematorium in the town, to be used with due regard to the interests of justice, and in fulfilment only of the testamentary instructions of such deceased persons as may have decided to be cremated. ("Lancet," 28th February, 1908.) 46 Norway. A Crematorium was opened at Bergen in June, 1907, during which month three bodies were cremated. Sweden. Crematoria were established in Stockholm and Gothenburg in the years 1887 and 1890 respectively, where up to the middle of 1907, 1,152 cremations had taken place. Switzerland. Basle, Geneva, St. Gall and Zurich are supplied with Crematoria, the first being built in the latter town in 1889. From that date to the middle of 1907, 3,129 cremations have taken place in this republic. United States of America. Washington, D.C., was the first town in the United States in which a Crematorium was introduced. It was built in 1876. The next were built at Lancaster Pa., and Washington Pa., in 1884, and since that date thirty similar establishments have been erected in other parts of the Union. The returns of the number of bodies incinerated are somewhat incomplete, but it is known that from 1876 to the middle of 1907, 335,732 cremations have been carried out in the States. Canada. The only Crematorium in Canada was opened in 1903, when up to the end of 1904, 44 bodies were dealt with. The returns for 1906-7 are not available. South America. Crematoria have been established at Rio de Janeiro, Buenos Ayres and Monte Video, but the number of bodies cremated are not given. In addition to the foregoing, Crematoria are in operation at Calcutta, India, Adelaide, South Australia; also one at Shanghai, China, for the use of Europeans. An attempt is being made by a local Cremation Society at Auckland, New Zealand, to induce the Municipality to establish a Crematorium. 47 With regard to the establishment of Crematoria in India, it is interesting to note that so long ago as March, 1874, Major Martin, of the 4th King's Own Regiment, issued from the Regimental Press at Poona, a "Specification of a Cinerator for the use of Brahmins and other Hindoo castes who perform their funeral rites by burning." Major Martin, in introducing the subject, remarks :— "Burning the dead having been from time immemorial a funeral rite "practised by certain castes in India, and one which it would not, from "its peculiar adaptation to the climate, be wise to endeavour to abolish ; "it becomes us to consider how the custom can be modified so as not to "prove a nuisance and cause of nuisance." "It is really time that measures were taken to remove from our sight "the disgusting scenes forced upon us in our daily walks, and to prevent "our health and that of the natives themselves being affected by the "pernicious vapours emanating from diseased bodies undergoing the "process of calcination." A full description and illustration of the proposed "Cinerator" is given, and this little book forms an interesting link in the bibliography relating to modern cremation. In view of the importance in the interests of the public health of encouraging the adoption of cremation, it would seem desirable that the question of reducing the cost of cremation at the City of London Cemetery should be considered. At the present time, the charge for cremation at the City of London Cemetery is £4 4s., this fee including Clergy fee in respect of a burial service, before, at, or after cremation, the .provision of a plain urn for the ashes, storage of the latter for three months, and at the expiration of this period, its burial in a plot of ground specially reserved for this purpose. 48 CREMATIONS IN THE UNITED KINGDOM, 23 Years—1885-1907. Vide " Transactions of Cremation Society of England, 1903," p. 31. 49 ARTIZANS' DWELLINGS, STONEY LANE, HOUNDSDITCH. These buildings, erected by the late Commissioners of Sewers and opened in January, 1885, stand upon ground formerly the site of several courts and alleys in the Ward of Portsoken. There are five "blocks" of Dwellings, severally named King's—Queen's— Prince's—North—and South, designed for occupation by persons of the artizan class ; some of the ground floors being let as shops. The buildings contain 501 rooms, arranged in 241 tenements, and are occupied by 941* persons, compared with 1,031 in 1906. Sixty-two per cent, are adults, and thirty-eight per cent, children (under 14 years). This reduction in the number of inhabitants is owing to the steps taken to prevent overcrowding, which had been brought to my notice in the course of the year. It should be stated that where overcrowding was found to exist, it was not due to the abuse of sub-letting but mainly to the natural increase of families. The death rate for 1907 was 13*8 per 1,000 per annum [of which 30*7 per cent, was of children under 5 years of age]. The number of deaths per 1,000 births of children under one year was 91, as against 89 for the City generally. With the exception of one year, viz., 1903, the expenditure for disinfectants was the lowest recorded since the methods and material adopted in 1901 have been in operation. This is due 'to the cold and wet summer season experienced in 1907. As heretofore, all the disinfectants used in the Public Health Department were obtained through your Medical Officer of Health. They are used for the following purposes :— A. Deodorizing and " denaturalizing " condemned meat. B. Disinfecting public conveniences (urinals, &c.). C. Disinfecting roadways, street gullies, &c. D. Disinfecting premises, &c., after infectious disease, drain testing and sundries. The amounts spent during the past twelve months under these several headings were :— £ s. d. During the year arrangements were made with the heads of the several other departments of the Corporation whereby in the future any disinfectants required by them can be obtained direct from the Contractor supplying the Public Health Department. It is hoped that a considerable economy will be effected by this procedure. 53 SANITARY INSPECTIONS.
