A list of registered tenement houses is given in Appendix C (p. 174). COMMON LODGING-HOUSES. 24-25, New Street, Bishopsgate. 4, Vine Street, Minories. These premises formerly used as common lodging-houses and closed in February, 1906, have since been demolished, and police dwellings erected on their sites. 116, Middlesex Street. The " Salvation Army Working Men's Hotel and Shelter," No. 116, Middlesex Street, is the only common lodging-house in the City. It provides sleeping accommodation for 453 persons, and was opened on the 7th February, 1906. On the 27th February, 1906, when reporting the opening of this place to the Sanitary Committee, I expressed the opinion that the establishment of a common lodging-house of such large dimensions within the City would probably result in an increase in the number of persons chargeable upon the ratepayers as destitute sick inmates removable to the City Infirmary. 58 In my annual report for last year some figures were given showing that this prediction was amply justified by events, and the number of inmates that have sought the shelter of the City Infirmary during 1907 has been even greater than in the previous year. During the twelve months ended 31st December, 1907, 391 persons (as against 303 in 1906) frequenting this place became chargeable upon the ratepayers. Of these 300 (against 210 in 1906) received orders for admission to the City of London Infirmary at Bow, 63 were given orders for admission to the City Workhouse at Homerton, and the remaining 28 received medical advice only, being not ill enough to require treatment at the Infirmary. A person has only to sleep one night in this place to qualify for admission to the City Infirmary, and this method of securing medical treatment or advice is becoming increasingly popular. In July, 1907, the Churchwardens and Overseers of St. Botolph Without, Bishopsgate, had occasion through their Chairman, Mr. Bull, C.C., to draw the attention of the Sanitary Committee to the matter with a view of protecting the interests of the Overseers and Ratepayers. This question of persons in quasi-philanthropic institutions becoming chargeable to the Ratepayers of the districts in which such places are situated has arisen elsewhere than in the City. " Bruce House," a County Council common lodging-house in the Strand, and a Rowton House in Hammersmith Road are also cases in point. With regard to the latter, it was reported by the Finance and General Purposes Committee at a meeting of the Hammersmith Guardians on 29th January last* that they had considered a statement and particulars submitted by the Clerk with reference to the cost of the cases admitted from Rowton House during the year ended at Michaelmas, 1906, and recommended that Rowton House, Ltd., be informed that the sum of £2,040, stated to be the gross cost to the rates in the Annual Report of the Local Government Board Inspector, is rather under than over the amount actually expended. Nor is this the only objection to such an institution in the City. Much annoyance was caused by some hundreds of undesirables congregating near the building between the hours of three and five every afternoon before they were admitted at the latter hour. These men conduct themselves in anything but an orderly manner, and complaints were also made of the nuisance caused by the freedom with which they expectorate on the footpaths. The assistance of the Commissioner of Police was invited with a view to remedying these evils, and the Army officials have directed applicants for * Daily Telegraph, 30th January, 1908. 59 accommodation not to assemble before 4 p.m., and they are now admitted an hour earlier, viz., 4 p.m. instead of 5 p.m. as heretofore. These alterations have effected considerable improvement in lessening the nuisance complained of. WATER-CLOSETS AND DRAINAGE PLANS. In the course of the year 341 plans for the proposed reconstruction of water-closets and drains were submitted for approval. With modification in certain cases, these were agreed to and the works carried out. In sixteen cases, however, it was found that plans for waterclosets were not in accordance with the Corporation Bye-laws, and were disallowed. OVERCROWDING. Overcrowding is a nuisance dealt with under Section 2, (1), (c) of the Public Health (London) Act, 1901, and nineteen cases were reported during the year. Notices of abatement being served, the overcrowding was discontinued in each instance. Several of these were discovered during a room to room inspection at the Artizans' Dwellings at Stoney Lane. LEGAL PROCEEDINGS. In seven cases only, out of 1,185 Notices issued during the year by the District Inspectors, was it found necessary in consequence of non-compliance to refer the matter to the City Solicitor with a view to taking such steps as he deemed necessary to ensure the required sanitary improvement being carried out. With one exception—now being dealt with—the Notices were subsequently complied with without the matter being taken into Court. The following are the cases referred to :— Premises. 1-14, Barbican - Drainage defects - Drainage relaid. 26, Cloth Fair - Non-registration of tenement house. Notice complied with. Bartholomew Place - Defective paving of private court. Court repaved. 50, Moorgate Street - Defective drain - In Solicitor's hands. 2, Shaftesbury Place - Overcrowding - Overcrowding abated. 36, Primrose Street - Defective drains and watercloset. Notice complied with. 29, Milk Street - Dry rot in floors of basement - Concrete floor substituted. 60 STREET GULLIES. Several complaints were received during the summer months of nuisance of smells from street gullies in the public thoroughfares. In dry weather this is sometimes due to want of flushing, but in a large number of cases the nuisance arises through faulty construction, many of these gullies being untrapped. The City Engineer is giving special attention to this matter, and where complaints are made owing to this cause these appliances are being reconstructed greatly to the relief of passers by and persons living in the vicinity. SEWER VENTILATORS IN ROADWAY'S. These also are from time to time the subject of complaint. It has been found that the best way—although a somewhat costly one—of abating the evil is to close the ventilators in the roadway and substitute large iron ventilating shafts attached to the wall of a neighbouring building. At times some little difficulty is experienced in obtaining the consent of the owner to the erection of the proposed shaft, but in most instances they are willing to render assistance towards the abatement of what is an intolerable nuisance under certain conditions of atmosphere, &c. LEADENHALL MARKET. The whole of the drainage throughout the market has been re-constructed and the system of drainage ventilation re-modelled. New soil and water-pipes have been fitted, and in some cases water-closets were removed from basements where they could not be efficiently ventilated. removal of fish offal. The contract with Messrs. F. S. Hemplieman and Co. for the removal of Fish Offal expired on the 21st May, 1907, and on the recommendation of the City Engineer and your Medical Officer of Health was renewed for a further period of twelve months. The conditions of the contract require that the Contractor collect and remove, free of charge, between the hours of 7 p.m. and 9 a.m., all fish offal from the Corporation Markets and Fishmongers' premises in the City, to the entire satisfaction of the Medical Officer of Health, in specially constructed galvanised iron bins, of a pattern approved by the Medical Officer of Health, and to thoroughly cleanse and disinfect such bins before returning them to the markets and shops. DRAINAGE, RED BULL WHARF, UPPER THAMES STREET. In the course of some building operations at this place, which has been acquired for the purpose of a cold storage depot, it was found that the drain from the Wharf also received the drainage from some premises in Red Bull Yard adjoining. 61 This arrangement being unsatisfactory, as it would have been most undesirable to have a drain running through premises where meat is stored and where it would be affected by the low temperature of the place, an arrangement was made by which the owners were enabled to divert the whole of the drainage into the public sewer in Angel Passage on the East side of the Wharf. DRAINAGE, ROYAL EXCHANGE. Considerable progress has been made during the year in the reconstruction of the drainage and sanitary arrangements at this building, and almost twothirds of the work has been completed and tested with satisfactory results. Owing to the construction of the buildings considerable difficulties have presented themselves in the course of the alterations. As an instance, in some places the workmen have had to cut through no less than nine feet of solid brickwork, the building being of massive construction. Piercing the stone mouldings, for the admission of ventilation shafts, so as not to spoil the architectural features of the building, was also a difficult and laborious operation. Iron pipes for soil drainage have been adopted throughout the Exchange, and the provision of sanitary accommodation for women employed in the building, referred to in my last Annual Report, has been arranged for. DRAINAGE, Nos. 1 to 14, BARBICAN. Complaints having been made of leakage through the railway tunnel over the Metropolitan Railway line running under these premises, it was found in the course of investigation that the main combined drain taking the drainage of these buildings was very defective and the cause of the evil. The several owners were therefore called upon to abate the nuisance, but some question having arisen as to their legal liability in the matter, it was considered advisable in the interests of all parties concerned to settle the matter amicably if possible. It was finally suggested that the Corporation should relay the drain and bear some portion of the charges incurred, the owners of the premises each bearing a proportionate share of the cost. This was agreed to, and the drain relaid, iron pipes being used as less liable to damage from vibration caused by railway traffic. RAILWAY STATIONS. Periodical inspections were made of the sanitary accommodation provided at the twenty-five Railway Stations in the City. In nineteen of them, the Companies they belong to have been called upon to effect sanitary improvements thereat. The defects, for the most part, have been due to lack of proper cleaning and to defective drains and sanitary fittings. In each case the necessary works have been satisfactorily carried out. 62 INSPECTION OF SCHOOLS. The undermentioned schools in the district have been inspected, various defects found, and the following works executed :— St. Bride's School, Bride Lane.—New water-closets, urinal, soil-pipe, and lavatory basins have been constructed. St. Dunstan's Schools, Fleet Street.—New iron drains, waterclosets, urinals, soil-pipe, and lavatory waste pipe constructed. St. Dunstan's Infants' School, Fetter Lane.—New iron drains, new water-closets, urinals, lavatory basins, waste pipes and soil-pipes have been constructed. The ventilation to the School-room has been improved, and a new lobby to the water-closets constructed. St. Bartholomew the Great Schools, Bartholomew Close.—The soilpipes were tested at the request of the Education Authority and found defective. The defects have been remedied, and the drains and soil-pipes again tested with water and found sound. REMOVAL OF HUMAN REMAINS. Four cases were dealt with in the course of the year, viz., at :— St. Peter-le-Poor. No. 9, Fore Street. St. Sepulchre Schools, Nos. 8 and 9, Giltspur Street. Site of Old Christ's Hospital. ST. PETER-LE-POOR. The Secretary of State, under circumstances fully detailed in my Annual Report for 1906, on the application of the Ecclesiastical Commissioners on behalf of the Vestry Clerk, having given his consent under Section 16 of the Union of Benefices Act, 1860, for the removal of human remains (under the direction of your Medical Officer) found during the demolition of this Church, which had been united with the benefice of St. Michael, Cornhill, the work of exhumation was commenced on the 18th of February, 1907, and completed on the 8th of April of that year. In the process, the earth covering the whole of the area of the Church was removed down to the virgin soil, and sifted for bones. These filled 252 boxes, which were taken to the City of London Cemetery at Ilford for re-interment in accordance with the regulations in force in the City for such removal. One other coffin claimed by relatives was removed and buried elsewhere. In all, 253 cases were buried at Ilford, 241 were interred in one enclosure in consecrated ground purchased for the purpose by the Ecclesiastical Commissioners, the remaining 11, by special desire, being buried in two private graves adjoining that in which the majority were placed. 63 ST. SEPULCHRE'S SCHOOLS, GILTSPUR STREET. In the course of some rebuilding operations at Nos. 8 and 9, Giltspur Street, adjoining St. Sepulchre's Schools, a quantity of human remains were disturbed. Upon application to the Home Secretary a license dated 11th February was issued authorising their re-interment in the City of London Cemetery, Ilford, under the supervision and to the satisfaction of your Medical Officer. The Churchwardens of St. Sepulchre subsequently made a formal request to be allowed to re-inter them on the spot in the burial ground belonging to the Church, from which at some time they had originally been removed. This was allowed, as it involved no danger to the public health and avoided 9 considerable expense. The bones were re-laid in the Churchyard, being surrounded and covered with a sufficient layer of properly made concrete. FROM THE SITE OF OLD CHRIST'S HOSPITAL. In April, 1907, during some excavations made on the site of Old Christ's Hospital for the extension of the General Post Office building, some human remains were found. These filled four cases, which were removed and re-interred at the City of London Cemetery at Ilford with the usual precautions. In this instance the bones were dealt with under the provisions of Section 7 of Christ's Hospital (London) Act, 1901, which provided that any human remains found on any part of the site of Christ's Hospital were to be removed under the superintendence and to the satisfaction of the Medical Officer of Health for the City of London. No. 9, FORE STREET. In September, 1907, in the course of some excavations required in the rebuilding of the above premises, which adjoined to the North-east corner of Cripplegate Churchyard, some human remains were discovered which, when collected, filled two cases. After communicating with the Home Office and the Churchwardens of Cripplegate, an arrangement was made for their reinterment in the Churchyard, and this was duly carried out under my supervision. 64 UNLAWFUL REMOVAL OF HUMAN REMAINS. In December last, it became necessary to call the attention of builders and other persons responsible for conducting building operations in the City, which are likely to involve any disturbance of human remains in excavating, to the importance of strict observance of the provision of Section 25 of the Burial Act, 1857, which expressly enacts that :— " Except in cases where a body is removed from one consecrated place " of burial to another by faculty granted by an ordinary for that purpose, " it shall not be lawful to remove any body, or the remains of any body, " which may have been interred in any place of burial, without license " under the hand of one of H.M. Principal Secretaries of State, and " with such precautions as such Secretary of State may prescribe as the " condition of such license ; and any person who shall remove any such " body or remains, contrary to this enactment, or who shall neglect to " observe the precautions prescribed as the condition of the license for " removal, shall, on summary conviction, before any two justices of the " peace, forfeit and pay for every such offence a sum not exceeding ten " pounds." The prohibition in this section extends to any place of burial, and not merely to burying grounds which have been closed, or to burying grounds provided by burial boards or otherwise. In the case of Reg. v. Sharpe, 26 L.J. (N.S.) M.C, 47 ; 7 Cox, C.C. 214, it was held to be an indictable misdemeanour at common law to enter an unconsecrated burial ground, and without the knowledge or consent of the owners of the ground, or authority of any kind, to dig up and carry away a corpse buried therein ; and this, even although the person committing the act may have been actuated by motives of affection and respect to the dead, and of religious duty, and have conducted the removal decently. The 15 and 16 Vict., c. 85, s. 42, enables places to be established for the reception of bodies until they are interred. (Glen's Burial Boards Act, 1881). As it did not appear that this requirement was generally known, the Committee, on my suggestion, directed the publication in the public press of the following notice :— Notice to Architects, Builders and others. " The attention of Architects, Builders, and other persons connected " with building operations in the City, which are likely to involve the " disturbance of human remains, is directed to the responsibilities " incurred by any breach of the following Section of the Burial Act of " 1857, and to the importance of at once giving notice to the Medical " Officer of Health of any cases where human remains are discovered :— 65 " ' Burial Act, 1857—Section 25. " ' Except in the cases where a body is removed from one " ' consecrated place of burial to another by faculty granted by the ' ordinary for that purpose, it shall not be lawful to remove any " ' body, or the remains of any body, which may have been interred " ' in any place of burial, without licence under the hand of one of " ' Her Majesty's Principal Secretaries of State, and with such '' ' precautions as such Secretary of State may prescribe as the " ' condition of such license ; and any person who shall remove any " ' such body or remains, contrary to this enactment, or who shall " ' neglect to observe the precautions prescribed as the condition of " ' the license for removal, shall, on summary conviction before any " ' two justices of the peace, forfeit and pay for every such offence " ' a sum not exceeding ten pounds." COMBINED DRAINAGE. For many years past, the law relating to Combined Drainage in the Metropolis has been in a most unsatisfactory state, and has cast an unduly heavy burden upon the ratepayers. The St. Pancras Borough Couucil have taken a very active part in endeavouring to remedy the present anomalous condition of the law, and have invited the support of the Corporation in their efforts to amend the legislation dealing with the question. Although, owing to their drainage works being mainly constructed under local Acts, the Corporation are not so immediately concerned in this matter as the Metropolitan Borough Councils, yet it was felt that the support of the Corporation should be extended to any movement for improving the existing condition of affairs, more especially as it has been estimated that the cost incurred by the ratepayers of London in reconstructing sewers due to combined drainage, has amounted during the three years 1904-6 to a sum of no less than £65,000, and it is certain that such liability will continue year by year as owners of property become better acquainted with the legal position, and therefore will be more ready to take advantage of the difficulties which local authorities experience in proving the liability of the owners for drainage works. By invitation of the St. Pancras Borough Council, a Conference of representatives of the City and Metropolitan Borough Councils was held at the St. Pancras Town Hall on the 27th June, 1907, to consider the whole matter, when it was referred to a Committee of the Conference to prepare for presentation to the President of the Local Government Board a Petition and Draft Bill dealing with the law relating to this question. 66 A second meeting was held on the 6th December, 1907, when the Committee's Report and Draft Bill was presented. It was a short Bill of two Clauses, Clause I being a definition of the meaning of the word " Drain," and Clause II. the short title. Clause I. was to the following effect:— 1. " In and for the purposes of the Metropolis Management Acts, " 1855 to 1899, and the Public Health (London) Act, 1891, or any Act " or Acts amending the same, the word " drain " shall be deemed to " include any sewer or drain, whether constructed before or after the " passing of this Act, with which two or more houses or premises " (whether belonging to the same or different owners) are at the date of " the passing of this Act, or may at any time thereafter, be connected or " which is used or capable of being or intended to be used for the " conveyance of the drainage of such houses or buildings, directly or by '' means of any other sewer or drain, to any public sewer, but shall not " include any sewer which has been constructed as a sewer to the satis" faction of the Corporation of the City of London, the London County " Council, the Metropolitan Borough Councils or their respective " predecessors, or any sewer which has been constructed by any of " those Authorities at the public expense." -Clause II. was as under :— 2. " This Act may be cited as the Metropolis Management Amend" ment Act, 1907, and shall be construed as one with the Metropolis " Management Act, 1855, and the Acts amending the same." The Committee recommended :— " (a) That the Draft Bill be approved and adopted." " (b) That a copy of the Draft Bill approved by the Conference be " forwarded to each of the City and Metropolitan Borough " Councils." " (c) That the City and Metropolitan Borough Councils be asked to " bring the Draft Bill before the Members of Parliament repre" senting their respective Districts, with a view to obtaining their " support and co-operation in the promotion of the Bill through " the various stages of the House." The report was received, when, upon the motion of the Chairman that recommendation (a) be adopted, an amendment was brought forward to the following effect:— " In and for the purposes of the Metropolis Management Acts, 1855 " to 1899, and the Public Health (London) Act, 1891, or any Act or 67 " Acts amending, the same word " drain" shall be deemed to include any " sewer or drain, whether constructed before or after the passing of this " Act, with which two or more houses or premises are at the date of the " passing of this Act, or may at any time thereafter, be connected or " which is used or capable of being or intended to be used for the " conveyance of the drainage of such houses or buildings, directly or " indirectly, to any sewer which has been approved as such by the " Metropolitan Commissioners of Sewers, the Metropolitan Board of " Works or the London County Council, but shall not include any sewer " so approved as aforesaid." Upon being put to the vote, the amendment was carried, as also when put as a substantive motion. Recommendations (6) and (c) were also agreed to. Should this Draft Bill meet with the support of the City and Metropolitan Borough Councils, no doubt steps will be taken to promote the Draft Bill as amended during the present Session. SANITARY DEFECTS AND INFECTIOUS DISEASE. All premises where infectious disease was notified were inspected with reference to drainage, soil and waste pipes, water-closets, water supply, ventilation, &c., and of the 106 cases, it has been necessary to entirely reconstruct the sanitary system in 10 instances ; and amendments have been made to soil and waste pipes and W.C's in 62 others. HOUSE REFUSE REMOVAL. The City Engineer informs me that the refuse removed from the City in 1907 consisted of 31,977 cartloads of street sweepings and slop, and 51,077 loads of house and trade refuse. Total, 83,054 loads. The use of the Destructor was discontinued in September, 1905, since which date all refuse has been removed by barge from Letts' Wharf to the Corporation land on Hornchurch Marshes, where it is dealt with. 68 SUMMER FLUSHING OF COURTS. All courts and narrow passages throughout the City were regularly flushed during the summer months under the direction of the City Engineer, who informs me that in this service and the washing of the main streets last year no less than 77,424,000 gallons of water were used. Disinfectants were also freely applied where necessary. The condition of the approaches to Billingsgate Market and the Custom House, as heretofore, required special attention, there having always been a difficulty in keeping the thoroughfares in the neighbourhood of these places free from the nuisance caused by the drippings from the fish carts, especially in hot weather. Crown Court, Whitefriars Little Montague Court, Little Wine Office Court, Fleet Street. Britain. Street. Dorset Court, Dorset Street. SMOKE NUISANCES. Smoke nuisances were reported at twelve premises during 1907. The number of observations recorded amounted to 341, as against 595 noted at 19 premises in 1906. The service of Notices upon offenders resulted in the abatement of the nuisances in all cases, and therefore, there has been no occasion to take further action in the matter. No nuisance of smoke from Railway locomotion was reported during the year. It is gratifying to note that at a discussion on " the Smoke problem in large towns" held at the Royal Sanitary Institute, on the 14th November, 1907, not only were the reports and efforts for dealing with this evil, of the late Sir John Simon, formerly Medical Officer of Health for the City, made 50 years ago, commended, but similar suggestions for effecting improvements as those mentioned in your Medical Officer's Annual Reports for 1903 and 1906 were also entirely concurred in. It was generally agreed that legislation was required for dealing with the following points:— (1) The substitution of some other term for the word " black" in in Section 24 (b) of the Public Health (London) Act, 1891. At present 72 unless the smoke causing the evil is " black smoke," it is not a nuisance that can be dealt with summarily under that Act. (2) Owners of private dwelling-houses should no longer be permitted to discharge smoke from their chimneys in such quantity as to be a nuisance. If stronger measures for dealing with smoke nuisances become operative it will greatly encourage an increased use of both gas and electricity for cooking purposes. There can be no doubt that the substitution of electric motors for steam engines in printing works and other places where power is used, has considerably lessened the smoke evil. HYGIENE OF HAIRDRESSERS' AND BARBERS' SHOPS. The number of registered Hairdressers' establishments remains about the same as in 1906, six new firms have been placed on the Register,.four have been closed, and in three cases the names have been struck off for noncompliance with the Regulations. During the year information was received that the Governor of Cape Colony, with the advice of the Executive Council, had approved the adoption of the Regulations in use in the City of London as Regulations for the control of Barbers' and Hairdressers' Saloons within the Borough of Kimberley. It appears that Colonial Authorities are armed with more effectual powers of insisting upon their requirements being carried out, than obtains in the mother country, as one of the Regulations in force in the Borough of Kimberley reads as under : — " Any person contravening any of the above Regulations shall be " liable on conviction to a fine not exceeding ten pounds sterling, and in " default of payment to imprisonment with or without hard labour for " any period not exceeding three calendar months." The action of a Colonial Authority in adopting the Regulations of the Corporation must be considered as complimentary, and the inhabitants oi Kimberley are to be congratulated upon the action of their representative in making any breach of their Regulations a punishable offence. It is much to be regretted that similar powers are not in operation here. 73 The Register of those Hairdressers who have undertaken to comply with the Regulations now includes the following establishments :— The Army and Navy Toilet Club, 35, Bucklersbury. LONDON COUNTY COUNCIL (GENERAL POWERS) BILL, 1907. This Bill, an abstract of which was given in my last Annual Report, received the Royal Assent on 28th August, 1907, and came into operation on the first day of January of this year. The Bill was opposed by the Corporation, as it particularly affected their interests. Opposition to the Bill was also presented from other quarters, and certain of the Council's proposals were abandoned. As originally drafted; the Bill contained much controversial matter and unsatisfactory proposals, which might have jeopardized the chances of its passing. 74 Of these the most important were Parts IV., V. and VI., relating to the milk supply of the Metropolis. Part IV.—Clauses 21 to 31, for the prevention of the supply of tuberculous milk to the county, was retained, and is now in operation. Part V.—Clauses 32 to 48, Miscellaneous Sanitary Provisions, was abandoned. Part VI.—Clauses 49 to 51, to legalise the establishment of Milk Depots in the Metropolis, was also dropped. The main objection to Part IV., which is intended to deal with tuberculous milk, is that it attacks the question at the wrong end, and seeks to deal with it at the railway station of delivery instead of at the source—the farm. Moreover, the London County Council has no power to stop the delivery of any consignment that may be under suspicion. They can take samples, but before the result of the examination can be known, the remainder of the milk will have been consumed. The Council have by this Act merely obtained power to exclude tuberculous milk from the County of London, and there is nothing to prevent the farmer selling infected milk elsewhere when excluded from the Metropolis. As originally drafted, the Bill debarred the Corporation from exercising the powers sought by the County Council in the City for the administration of this part of the Act, but when the Bill was in Committee in the Lords, the Corporation secured the addition of a Clause conferring on them or their Medical Officer similar powers within the City concurrently with the County Council or their Medical Officer. Part V. of the Bill, as drafted, gave the County Council powers to deal with unwholesome or dirty milk in a very similar way to those obtained in Part IV. with regard to tuberculous milk, but owing to the opposition of the Corporation and others, it was dropped during the progress of the Bill in Parliament. It is revived again, however, in Part II. of the General Powers Bill, introduced by the County Council this Session (1908).* Part VI. contained proposals for granting authorities power to establish depots in their districts for the sale of sterilized milk. This also was not preceeded with by the Council. Apart from the question of municipal trading, which involved competition with local tradesmen at a loss to the ratepayers, as evidenced by similar experiments in Lambeth, *On the 6th March, while this Report was in the press, the London County Council (General Powers) Bill, 1908, was read a second time in the House of Commons when, on the President of the Local Government Board giving an undertaking to bring in a general Bill dealing with tha milk supply of the whole country, an instruction was given to the Committee to strike out all the clauses in Part II. of the Bill dealing with the milk supply other than the model milk clauses. The opposition of the Corporation to this part of the Bill is thus fully justified by the action of the legislature. 75 Woolwich, and Battersea, there is no evidence that sterilization is the most effectual way to reduce infantile mortality, indeed the contrary is the generally accepted view of those who have carefully followed up the question. We know as a matter of fact that rather more than eight per cent. of the milk supplied to the City contains the bacillus of tuberculosis. Tuberculous milk is diseased milk, and is known to be potent for danger to life, as shown by the Local Government Board Report on Tuberculosis (see p. 37 of this report). It is obvious that it is far better to eliminate any risk of danger at its source, than to adopt doubtful expedients for rendering milk harmless after it has passed out of the control of the farmer. The observance of strict cleanliness in the handling of milk from the cow to the child, together with the taking of precautions to prevent the use of tubercular cows for dairy purposes, is the more rational course to adopt. The Corporation further petitioned against the sections relating to the Cleansing of Verminous Persons and their Clothing in Common LodgingHouses, which powers, it was proposed, should be exercised by the County Council Officers within the City. This petition was successfully sustained, and similar powers to those sought by the County Council have been conferred upon the Corporation within the City in which the Council's Officers will not have any authority to exercise the powers sought. There are great objections to the promotion of legislation dealing with the control of food supplies to the Metropolis and elsewhere, by patchwork legislation of the kind promoted by the London County Council, whose General Powers Bills generally contain proposals that are extensions or amendments of the Public Health Acts. The latter being a class of legislation which should more properly be promoted by the Government of the day, and be made to apply to the whole country. The control of milk supplies is especially a matter that should be under the supreme authority of the Board of Agriculture and Fisheries, whose business it should be to see that the Dairies, Cowsheds and Milkshops Orders are effectively carried out by those responsible for their administration. The need for more radical improvement in this direction is shown by the views expressed on this matter in the last published Annual Report of the Local Government Board (1906-1907), quoted on page 108 (Dairies and Milkshops) of this Report. Some further notes on this subject will be found on p. 24 (Contaminated Milk). 76 FACTORY AND WORKSHOP ACT, 1901. FACTORIES, WORKSHOPS AND WORKPLACES. Since the year 1902, when the above Act came into operation, the work of inspection has continuously increased. The Register of Workshops compiled by H.M. Factory Inspectors contained the names of 1,452 firms, and this number has since been increased to 1,927; while the Staff, which has been increased to five, is still insufficient to cope efficiently with it. The work of inspection is a laborious one, and the character of the Register continually changes from year to year owing to additions, removals, changes of name and description of the several businesses registered, and needs continuous attention to ensure a correct record being kept. It is not unusual for workshops to develop into factories, or vice versa, entailing elimination from one register and re-registration in the other. In addition to the figures before mentioned, 524 firms are now registered in the Factory Register, 837 in the Register of Kitchens of Restaurants and Tea Shops, and 103 in the Register of Workplaces (other than kitchens) where food is stored or prepared, &c. During the year 4,643 entries were made in the book recording references to the Inspectors concerned, together with letters received, &c.—16,570 names and addresses of outworkers were sent to other local authorities, and 2,643 notices were issued. This is in addition to correspondence, and abstracting necessary information from the Registers as required by the Act for the information of H.M. Factory Department, a work which absorbs much time and attention. The number of Factories, Workshops, and Workplaces at present registered is as follows :— Factories Premises. WORKSHOP As mentioned in previous Reports, the sanitary defects generally found in workshops may be classified under the following headings :— Want of cleansing. Insufficient ventilation. Overcrowding. The lack of separate and suitable water-closet acccommodation for both sexes. Much difficulty is experienced in dealing with the subject of ventilation where the employment of gas rings and stoves, &c., is the polluting agent. In many cases the only effectual remedy is mechanical ventilation, but this is not always easy of application. Where chimney openings exist it is well to place stoves or rings within them, and this has often been done on the suggestion of your Officers. Injury to health is at times caused by defective rubber tubing allowing gas to escape in small quantities, possibly unnoticed by the workers. Both in the interests of health and for the prevention of fire, this kind of pipe connection to gas rings should not be permitted. * Details of Workshop Inspections, 1907. SANITARY DEFECTS IN FACTORIES AND WORKSHOPS NOTIFIED IN 1907 BY H.M. INSPECTOR OF FACTORIES. Where sanitary defects (which are remediable under the Public Health Act, 1891, but not under the Factory and Workshop Act, 1901) are found by H.M. Factory Inspector in the course of his work, jt is his duty to notify the Local Authority of the same. During the year 21 such notifications were received, some of which referred to factories as well as workshops. The number is much below the average of the last 11 years. Some of the defects reported were of a minor character, want of icleansing, &c. The Factory Inspector was notified of defects in factories coming under the observation of the Workshop Inspectors. The sanitary defects reported at the 21 places referred to above were remedied before the end of the year. (2) Gas stoves, &c., not ventilated. (3) Defective drains. (4) Defective soil pipes. (5) Defective urinals. (6) Defective waste pipes, &c. OUTWORKERS AND "HOME" WORKERS. It is obvious that there can be but few " Home" workers in the City. The total number of Outworkers' premises registered is 197, only 49 of which can in any way be described as occupied by " Home " workers. The remainder are ordinary workshops, and appear on the Register of Workshops, The " Home " workers are visited by your Women Inspectors periodically. See page 102. In pursuance of Sections 107, 108 and 110 of the Factory and Workshop Act, 1901, the Home Secretary on the 23rd May, 1907, issued the following Order with regard to Home Work :— " I.—Section 107 (relating to lists of outworkers) and Section 108 (relating to " employment in unwholesome premises) shall apply to the following classes of " Work :— " The making, cleaning, washing, altering, ornamenting, finishing and repairs " ing of wearing apparel; " The making, ornamenting, mending and finishing of lace and of lace curtain and nets; " Cabinet and furniture making and upholstery work ; " The making of electro-plate ; " The making of files ; " Fur-pulling ; " The making of iron and steel cables and chains ; " The making of iron and steel anchors and grapnels ; " The making of cart gear, including swivels, rings, loops, gear buckles, " mullin bits, hooks and attachments of all kinds ; " The making of locks, latches and keys ; " The making or repairing of umbrellas, sunshades, parasols or parts thereof; " The making of artificial flowers; 85 " The making of nets other than wire nets; " The making of tents ; " The making or repairing of sacks ; " The covering of racquet or tennis balls; " The making of paper bags ; " The making of boxes or other receptacles or parts thereof made wholly or " partially of paper, cardboard, chip or other similar material. The Order issued prior to the above with regard to this question was dated loth August, 1905, and that now under review differs only so far as the addition of trades which experience has proved could with advantage be added to the list. These trades are printed in italics. The principal object aimed at in the outworker Clauses of the Act is, of course, the prevention of the spread of disease, and it may be of interest to explain the system in its operation. Persons engaged in certain specified trades are required to send to the Local Authority in which their premises are situated a list of the names and addresses of all persons employed by them as outworkers. These are forwarded by the Local Authority to the Borough or District Council in whose district the outworkers reside or have their place of business ; they are then registered by them, and in the event of any case of infectious disease occurring at the premises of an outworker, the fact is forthwith communicated to the employer, who is required to cease giving out work to the outworker and receiving work from him, until such time as the local Medical Officer of Health certifies the premises, <fcc., to be free from infection. Much difficulty was at first experienced in procuring lists from employers, and it was only after printed notice was issued to each firm concerned a few days before the Lists became due that an improvement took placed. A recent decision in the High Court has placed retail florists within the provisions of Section 149 of the Factory and Workshop Act, 1901. This case, Hoare v. Robert Green, Ltd., was an appeal from the decision of a metropolitan magistrate on a summons issued against the respondents for failing to exhibit in their workshop the prescribed abstract of the Act which is required by Section 128. The respondents carried on the business of retail florists, employing a number of young women and girls, whose duties consisted partly of serving customers in the shop and partly of making up natural flowers into wreaths, crosses and bouquets, and arranging floral decorations, for which purpose frames of wood or wire were sometimes used, in a room behind the shop. 90 It was contended on be'ialf of the respondents that in these circumstances they were not a workshop, and did not come within the provisions of Section 149 of the Factory and Workshop Act, 1901, and the Magistrate holding that the room was not a workshop within the meaning of the Act, the case was dismissed. On the application of the Inspector a case was stated for the opinion of the High Court, which was heard before the Divisional Court on the 26th July last, when the decision of the Magistrate was reversed, the Court holding that the room was a place in which manual labour was exercised by way of trade in or incidental to the making of an article or the adapting for sale of an article, within Section 149 of the Factory and Workshop Act, 1901, and that the room was therefore a workshop within the Act. There are sixteen of these places in the City, all of which have been inspected and registered as workshops. A few defects, including in some instances want of proper sanitary accommodation, were found to exist, but all are now being remedied. WORKPLACES (KITCHENS). The result of the inspection of basement kitchens is to show the entire unsuitability of most of these places for the purpose. The difficulty of efficiently ventilating underground rooms of any kind is very great, and it is obvious that this difficulty is much increased when to the ordinary vitiation of the air by breathing, are added the fumes from gas stoves and the steam generated in cooking. The most elaborate arrangement of air shafts and fans will scarcely avail to render the atmosphere fit to breathe, to say nothing of the unhealthy effect it is likely to have on the food cooked and stored in it. It has been found that in many basement kitchens a much greater amount of cooking is being done than was formerly the case, and Consequently that the number of gas stoves has increased in proportion, while no additional means of ventilation nor methods of carrying off the fumes from the stoves has been provided. In the 188 kitchens inspected during the year, no fewer than 132 defects have been found : 52 kitchens were badly ventilated ; 34 required cleansing ; in 31 cases the sanitary accommodation was dirty or defective ; and there were besides, 15 general defects. The number of women employed in these kitchens is 2,664. 91 The London County Council (General Powers) Bill, 1908, includes a clause dealing with the contamination of food in any premises in which " any " article, whether solid or liquid, intended or adapted for the food of man, is " sold or exposed for sale or deposited for the purpose of sale or of preparation " for sale or with a view to future sale." Clause 21 of this Bill provides that:— (a) " No urinal, water closet, earth closet, privy, ashpit, or other like sanitary convenience shall be within, or directly communicate with, such room or place ; (b) " No cistern for supplying water to such room or place shall be in direct communication with, and directly discharge into, any such sanitary convenience ; (c) " ISlo drain or pipe for carrying oft' fcecal or sewage matter shall have any inlet or opening within such room or place ; (d) " No such room or place shall be used as a sleeping place, and so far as may be reasonably necessary, to prevent risk of infection or contamination of any such article, no sleeping place shall adjoin and communicate directly with such room or place ; (e) " Refuse or filth, whether solid or liquid, shall not be deposited or allowed to accumulate in any such room or place ; (/) "No act or thing shall be done or permitted in any such room or place likely to expose any such article to infection or contamination." FACTORY AND WORKSHOP ACT, 1907. This Act was presented by Earl Beauchamp in the House of Lords on the 2nd May last, and after being amended in the House of Commons, was passed in both Houses and received the Royal Assent on the 28th August, 1907. The main object of the Act is :— To extend the provisions of the Factory and Workshop Act, 1901, to laundries carried on by way of trade or for purposes of gain, or carried on as ancillary to another business, or incidentally to the purposes of any public institution, and To regulate hours of employment in laundries ancillary to a business carried on in any premises which, apart from the provisions of this Act, are a factory or workshop. 92 The principle of extending legislation to institutional laundries has already been fully reported on in the Annual Reports, 1905 and 1906. As regards other laundries, the new Act does not make many changes in the duties imposed on local authorities. Hotel laundries are for the first time definitely included in the list of premises coming within the provisions of the Factory and Workshop Acts*, and laundries " in which not more than two persons dwelling elsewhere are employed " are no longer exempt. There is likely to be some confusion caused by the provisions of section 3 of the new Act, which refer to " workshop" laundries. Sub-section (6) requires " in every laundry, all stoves for heating irons to be sufficiently " separated from any ironing room or ironing table, and gas irons emitting " any noxious fumes are not to be used " ; and (c) " the floors to be kept in " good condition and drained in such a manner as will allow the water to flow " off freely." A laundry in which there is a contravention of any of these provisions shall be deemed to be a factory or workshop not kept in conformity with the principal Act. This places the duty of carrying out these provisions in the hands of H.M. Inspectors, regardless of the fact that under sections 7 and 8 of the Factory and Workshop Act, 1901 (which have not been repealed) contraventions of the provisions with regard to ventilation and drainage of floors in workshops are to be deemed to be nuisances liable to be dealt with summarily under the law relating to public health. And by section 1 of the new Act, laundries in which no mechanical power is used, are scheduled as workshops. There are consequently now two authorities responsible for the carrying out of these provisions. INSTRUCTION OF WOMEN INSPECTORS IN YIDDISH. During the year, with the approval of the Court, the two Women Inspectors (Miss Safford and Miss Pole) have attended classes for instruction in Yiddish, and the knowledge thus acquired has been found most useful in carrying out their duties among the foreign Jews dwelling in the neighbourhood of Houndsditch. * In a case taken under the Factory and Workshop Act, 1901 (Caledonian Railway Company v. Paterson, 1898, 1 F. (J. C.) 24), it was held that a laundry attached to a hotel was not carried on by way of trade or for purposes of gain when the following kinds of work were done in the laundry : (a) washing the hotel linen, (£>) washing the clothes of the hotel servants without charge as part of their remuneration, and (c) washing the clotheB of visitors to the hotel at high charges. 93 DOMESTIC WORKSHOPS, 1907. During the year 1907, 268 births were registered, 39 of which took place in public institutions, the parents being non-resident in the City. These cases have been notified to the Medical Officer of Health of the district to which they belong. In all cases where the notification of a birth is received and the parents reside in the City, the home is visited and the mother advised as to the care and feeding of the infant. Special attention is paid to the sanitary condition of the house, and notes taken as to the number of rooms occupied by the family, the number of persons living and sleeping in those rooms, whether the child sleeps with the parent or in a cot, and whether the mother goes out to work. Any sanitary defects found are handed over to the District Inspector to be dealt with. During the year 39 defects have been discovered, chiefly relating to defective sanitary arrangements, dirty walls and ceilings (in several cases verminous), and overcrowding. The total number of visits paid during the year was 657. 98 At the age of six months the infants are re-visited, and again at the age of twelve months, so as to obtain full particulars as to the methods of feeding during the first year of the child's life. These complaints were referred to the Milk Shop Inspector to be dealt with. The number of deaths of infants under twelve months registered during the year was 24. Details of these deaths are given in the Table on page 17. The following particulars were obtained as regards the feeding of the infants :— Seven were breast-fed entirely. One was fed on the breast and Nestle's Milk. Nine were having cow's milk either entirely or supplemented by the breast, Nestle's or other food. Three infants died within three days of birth. In four cases satisfactory particulars could not be obtained, the mothers having either returned to work or removed from the address given. It should be borne in mind, in considering this question of infant feeding, that a large number of children die every year from marasmus and debility, and that the cause of this mortality may frequently be traced to the deficient quality of the milk secreted by the mother. When there is poverty in the home, it is the mother and baby who suffer most, .as the husband and other children must be fed, so the woman herself goes without. Further, she is not only often underfed before the baby is born, but also all the time she is suckling it, consequently her milk becomes poor and unsatisfying, the infant fails to thrive, and gradually dies from malnutrition for lack of nature's food. In such cases, if only help in the way of providing good milk and other nourishment could be extended to the mother both before and after confinement, it would often be the means of ensuring greater health and strength for these little ones, and in many cases saving the life of a child. NOTIFICATION OF BIRTHS ACT, 1907. On the 23rd April, 1907, an Act, known as the Notification of Births Act, 1907, was brought in by Lord Robert Cecil, and received the Royal Assent on the 28th August, 1907. The object of this Act is to ensure early notification of births to the Medical Officer of Health, so that advice may be given to the mother in regard to the rearing and nurture of the child. % The Act may be adopted by any Local Authority in the United Kingdom. 100 Section 3 of the Act enables the Board by Order to declare the Act to be in force in the area of any Local Authority which has not so adopted, if they think this expedient, having regard to the circumstance of the area, and in that case the order of the Board will have the same effect for the purpose as a resolution of adoption duly passed by the Local Authority of the area, and assented to by the Board. By section 2, sub-section 3, it shall be the duty of any Local Authority by whom this Act may be adopted as soon as the consent of the Local Government Board is given to the resolution of adoption, to bring the provision of this Act to the attention of all Medical Practitioners and Midwives practising in their area. In the case of every child born within an area in which the Act is in force, it will be the duty of the father of the child, if he is actually residing in the house where the birth takes place at the time of its occurrence, and of any person in attendance upon the mother at the time of or within six hours of the birth, to give notice in writing of the birth to the Medical Officer of Health of the District in which the child is born. The Act provides for the registi'ation of still births. This is a most important point, as hitherto there has been no means of obtaining any record of these. The notice is to be given by posting a prepaid letter or post card addressed to the Medical Officer of Health giving the necessary information of the birth within 36 hours after the birth, or by delivering a written notice of the birth at the office of the Medical Officer within the same time. The Local Authority are required to supply, without charge, addressed stamped post cards containing forms of notice to any Medical Practitioner or Midwife residing or practising in the area who applies for them. A person who fails to give the requisite notice of a birth will be liable on summary conviction to a penalty not exceeding 20s., unless he satisfies the. Court that he had reasonable grounds to believe that notice had been duly given by some other person. The notification is to be in addition to, and not in substitution for, the requirements of any Act relating to the registration of birth. In London, the Medical Officer of Health of the City and of every Metropolitan Borough in which the Act is in force for the time being must send weekly to the London County Council, in a form prescribed by the Board, a list of all notices received by him under the Act during the past week. The Local Government Board consented to the adoption of this Act within the City of London, and fixed the 19th February, 1908, as the date for the Act to come into operation. 101 INDUSTRIAL EMPLOYMENT OF WOMEN. On 6th November last, a Conference was held at the Home Office to consider the question of further regulation of Industrial Employment of Women before and after child-birth. At the present time no woman is allowed to be employed in a factory or workshop within four weeks after she has given birth to a child. The Conference on Infantile Mortality advocated :— (1) The extension of this period from one month to three ; and (2) The prohibition of the employment of women advanced in pregnancy, unless medically certified to be fit for work. Before introducing such legislation the Home Secretary is anxious to be sure of his ground and to be satisfied that there is sufficient justification for imposing further restriction. He has, therefore, asked the Sanitary Authorities throughout the country to undertake an exhaustive enquiry into the whole subject, and to furnish returns which could be summarised and considered by the Home Office. The Sanitary Committee has agreed to make the proposed investigation, which is to extend over a period of two years. A supply of forms of enquiry have been received from the Home Office dealing with the questions of :— (1) Precise occupation of mothers during pregnancy. (2) How long ceased from work before birth of child. (3) How soon employed after confinement. (4) If the work carried on was in a factory, workshop, at home, or elsewhere. (5) Nature of the work—(1) laborious, or (2) light. (6) Feeding of the child. (7) Weekly earnings of the husband. (8) Income of family. And several other minor details. These forms when filled up with the necessary particulars are returned to the Home Office. The earlier notification of births will greatly facilitate this enquiry, as immediate information will now reach the Medical Officer of Health, and the information will extend to still-births as well as live births. 102 HOME WORK. A Select Committee of the House of Commons was appointed in May, 1907, " to consider and report upon the conditions of labour in trades in which " Home Work is prevalent, and the proposals, including those for the "establishment of Wages Boards, and the licensing of Work-places, which " have been made for the remedying of existing abuses." Your Worshipful Committee on 24th June, 1907, authorised Miss Safford (your senior Woman Inspector) to give evidence before the Select Committee. Miss Safford appeared before the Committee on the 25th July, and the following is an outline of the evidence she gave. CITY OF LONDON (1906). Outworkers' Lists.—The number of lists received from Employers during 1906 was 1,112, giving a total of 14,573 names of outworkers. Of these, 14,376 names were forwarded to other local authorities, leaving 197 as resident in the City. To these must be added 32 notified to the City by the local authorities. Of the 229 names received, 142 were notified by 1 employer, 47 by 2, 18 by 3, 8 by 4, 9 by 5, 2 by 6, 2 by 7, 1 by 11. Procedure as to Lists.—In January and July, notices were sent to all firms known to give outwork. In 1906, the 351 firms who neglected to send in a list by February and August 1st, received a cautionary letter from the City Solicitor. One firm failing to comply was summoned and fined. Outworkers working in the City.—Only 27*9 per cent, are Home Workers. Figures for 1906 show that of 229 Outworkers, 6 are Contractors, 5 are Factories, 154 are Workshops in premises other than Dwelling-houses 24 are Workshops in Dwelling-houses, 30 are Domestic Workshops, 10 Dwelling-houses in which work is taken in occasionally. The actual number of Home Workers is 64. In 31 cases the work is done in rooms used only for work. In 20 cases the work is done in the living room. In 13 cases the work is done in living and sleeping room. In the last class, 6 are rooms with only one occupant ; in two cases the occupants are a woman and an aged relative whom she is helping to support. 103 In one, the occupants are an old man and wife. In one, a woman living alone and employing a young girl, the remaining three are occupied by families where the mother was taking in work to supplement the father's wages. These three were all occasional workers. One room was overcrowded.
