A further Memorandum, on the Home Work Provisions of the Factory Act, was issued to all District 108 DAIRIES, COWSHEDS AND MILKSHOPS ORDERS, 1885, 1886 and 1899. Under these Orders every Local Authority is required to keep a Register of every purveyor of milk, dairyman, or cowkeeper in its district, to supervise the milk trade, and to carry out certain general regulations prescribed in the several Orders. Under these now in force the Local Authorities are bound to register any purveyor of milk no matter how unsuitable the premises may be where the milk is sold. The London County Council have deposited a General Powers Bill in which power is sought inter alia :— " To enable the Sanitary Authorities in London to refuse to register, " or to remove from the Register, Cowkeepers, Dairymen or Purveyors " of milk, whose premises are unsuitable for the sale of milk thereon." I have already reported at length to the Sanitary Committee on the 23rd April, 1907, upon a similar suggestion of the Metropolitan Borough of Holborn, and expressed therein the opinion that it appeared that there was nothing to prevent the Local Government Board making Orders prescribing, as a precautionary measure, that milk should not be sold on unsuitable premises, and this would, as a consequence, prohibit the registration of such places. Should this view be correct, any further statutory powers in this direction are unnecessary. As the whole question of regulation of food supplies throughout the country is now engaging the attention of the Local Government Board, it is very desirable that any additional powers for control of milk supplies should be placed in the hands of all Local Authorities, and further, that steps should be taken to ensure that they are strictly enforced everywhere. Nothing could be more unsatisfactory than the present manner in which many Authorities neglect their duties in this respect, as will be seen by some observations in this Report on " Contamination of Milk," pp. 24-30. This view of the matter is also supported by the last published Annual Report of the Local Government Board (1906-1907), as will be seen from the following extract:— " It is evident that many Local Authorities, especially Rural District " Councils, carry out the duties imposed upon them by the Dairies " Orders very imperfectly, and that even in districts where milk is " produced in large quantities and sent away by rail, insufficient attention " is paid to the cattle and their surroundings and to the sanitary " condition of the cowsheds. Only a few Rural District Councils " appear to attempt any system of Veterinary examination of cattle." 109 There are no cowsheds in the City of London. At the end of the year, 606 purveyors of milk were on the Register, as ■compared with 581 in the previous year, being an increase of 25. In the course of the year the sale of milk was discontinued at 30 premises and the owners' names removed from the Register. 55 new premises were added, and 34 changes of ownership, necessitating re-registrations, occurred. These premises are regularly and systematically inspected, special care being taken to ensure that milk receptacles are kept covered, and that the milk vessels used are kept in a cleanly condition. In the course of the year 55 cases were reported of neglect to keep pans used for the storage of milk covered in accordance with No. 17 of the City Regulations as to Milk Stores and Milk Shops. In each case the offender was cautioned. An early morning visit to some premises kept by an Italian resulted in finding him serving milk from a dirty receptacle. The man was warned, and the milk destroyed. Two cases of Infectious Disease (both Scarlet Fever) occurred on registered premises in the course of the year, and in each instance the patient was removed to hospital, and the necessary cleansing and disinfection carried out before allowing any distribution of milk from the premises. ICE CREAMS. Under the provisions of the Ice Cream, Section Nos. 42, 43 and 44 of the London County Council (General Powers) Act, 1902, which became operative on 1st November, 1903, any person engaged in the manufacture, sale or storage of ice cream, or any such commodity, who does any act or thing likely to expose such commodity to infection or contamination, or omits to take any proper precaution for the due protection of such commodity from infection or contamination, shall be liable for every such offence, on conviction, to a penalty not exceeding forty shillings. He is also liable to a similar penalty if he omits to notify the outbreak of any infectious disease amongst persons employed by him in the sale, manufacture or storage of such commodity. An itinerant vendor of ice cream, &c., must exhibit a notice on his barrow stating the name and address of the person from whom he obtains such articles, failing which, he also incurs a penalty not exceeding forty shillings. In 1907 there were 207 premises in the City where ice creams were sold, as compared with 233 in 1906, and 249 in 1905. 110 These are classified as under, the corresponding figures for 1906 being given in italics :— Places where Sold in City. Place of In the City. Manufacture. In other Districts. Tea shops 1907. 84 1906. 121 1907. 1906. 31 1907. 84 1908. 90 Confectioners 19 19 9 9 10 10 Restaurants 7 6 7 4 — 2 Small confectioners and light refreshment shops 97 87 41 29 — 58 Total 207 233 57 73 150 160 Shops owned by foreigners 88 79 32 22 • 56 57 Of the 97 small confectioners and light refreshment shops in the City who sold ice creams, 88 or 90*7 per cent, were owned by foreigners, compared with 90*8 per cent, in 1906. There was a slight increase of the number of itinerant ice cream vendors in the City last year, 40 being noted, as compared with 32 in 1906. No case was reported of a barrow being found not bearing the name and address of the manufacturers of the article sold. This was doubtless due to the prosecution, in 1906, of eight itinerant vendors, who failed to exhibit such notice as required by the Act. It was found that in every case the ice creams sold by itinerant merchants were made outside the City. During 1907 there were 57 places in the City where ice creams were manufactured, against 73 in 1906, the owners of the remaining 150 premises in the City dealing in this article obtaining it from manufacturers in other districts. Upon making enquiries of authorities outside the City with regard to the latter, it was reported that in six cases sanitary improvements were required, and in each instance the sale of ice cream from the places in question was prohibited until the necessary sanitary improvements had been carried out. All ice cream manufactured in the City was prepared under satisfactory conditions, with one exception, where it was made at a small tenement house that was quite unfit for the purpose ; upon being served with a notice to discontinue the manufacture of ice cream on the premises, the owner at once transferred his business to Stepney. Ill POISONOUS NUTS. An unusual case of poisoning occurred last summer, when on the 4th of August I received notice from the Police that several children at Guy's Hospital were being treated there for symptoms of irritant poisoning, caused by eating nuts sold by a costermonger in Peckham. The Hospital Authorities communicated with the Metropolitan Police, who, in turn, notified the City Police by telegram, as under :— " Several persons have been treated at Guy's Hospital and elsewhere " for vegetable poisoning, the result of eating Kaloo Nuts sold by " Mr. B., Duke's Place, Aldgate, to E. T., Blue Anchor Lane, Peckham. " 3rd inst." Upon receipt of this message, I visited the City and traced the bulk of the nuts to the premises of a cocoanut and general fruit merchant in Mitre Square, Aldgate, and obtained the surrender of the remainder of the consignment. On the following day, viz., Monday, 5th August (Bank Holiday), these nuts were taken before Alderman Sir John Ritchie at the Mansion House, who condemned the same as unfit for human food, and made an order for their destruction. It appears that they were a consignment of 16 cwt., of a kind unknown to the dealer, who had purchased them from a ship's captain who had brought them to this country as a speculation. There was said to be nothing exceptional in this transaction, as the dealer stated he was in the habit of encouraging ships' captains to furnish him with novelties of this kind. Fortunately the Peckham costermonger was the City dealer's first and only customer. This man bought two cwt., which he hawked in Southwark, with the result that five children, who ate some, had to be taken to Guy's Hospital. In addition to these, eight other children, belonging to five families living in Peckham, suffered from similar symptoms of irritant poisoning. In these cases, some boys from houses adjoining that where the nuts were stored had climbed a fence and stolen some of the nuts, which they subsequently distributed amongst their acquaintances. Dr. Newton Palmer, House Physician in Guy's Hospital, has kindly furnished me with the following notes on the cases seen by him : — " I was called to four of the five cases which came up, all presenting similar " features, their ages varying from two to seven years. The worst case (which " was admitted) was a child of two years eight months, suffering like the rest from " an acute gastritis. At 12 mid-day she had eaten some of these nuts, commenced " vomiting at ] p.m., and continued till after admission at 3 p.m. She was in a " collapsed condition, temp. 94'6, pulse 120, resp. 26, with sunken eyes and 112 "greyish face, the usual expression of an infant with acute vomiting. When "saw the child she was vomiting at fairly frequent intervals, apparently without "pain, watery bile-tinged fluid. There was no diarrhoea. After treatment she "rapidly recovered. The other children did not appear again at the surgery, "though told to do so, whence I infer that they rapidly improved." The Medical Officer of Health for Camberwell states that the symptoms in those attacked by illness in his district varied from severe pains in the stomach, diarrhoea, sickness and headache to a slight amount of indisposition. As there appears to have been some doubt as to the species of nut in question, it may be interesting to quote from the following report kindly sent by the Director of the Royal Botanic Gardens, Kew, to whom specimens had been submitted:— "The seeds received for identification are those of a species of Aleurites, probably "Aleurites Fordii, a native of China. The seeds of Aleurites Fordii are believed "to be the chief source of Chinese Wood Oil, and to possess poisonous properties. "The tree is closely allied to the Aleurites Triloba, a native of Malay and Polynesia, "the seeds of which are known as 'Indian Walnut' or 'Candle Nut,' and said to "contain 50 per cent, of oil used as food and for burning. "Royal Botanic Gardens, Kew, "August 6th, 1907." A sample was also sent to your Public Analyst for chemical examination, who reported thereon as follows:— "These nuts yield an oil agreeing in properties with Chinese Wood Oil obtained "from Aleurites Candata. It has the reputation of possessing poisonous properties. "17 th September, 1907." In No. 4, 1906, of the "Bulletin of Miscellaneous Information," published by the authorities at Kew Gardens, it is stated:— "With regard to the use of T'ung Oil in Europe and America, it is known that "it is employed to a considerable extent in one or two industries, and is also being "experimented with in others, but much secrecy is maintained with regard to the "matter. So far as can be gathered, it is employed in linoleum factories, as a "substitute for linseed in some industries, and is also believed to form the basis of "a varnish to compete with that produced from copal. It may be noted that this "oil has poisonous properties ; the refuse cake is employed as manure in China." The nuts of Aleurites Fordii have also been the subject of enquiry by the authorities of the Imperial Institute. The following notes are extracted from the Bulletin of the Imperial Institute, Vol. v., No. 2, 1907:— "Examination of the oil.—On extraction with light petroleum, the kernels were "found to contain 58.3 per cent, of oil, which is equivalent to a yield of 36.4 per "cent, from the entire nuts. "The oil was light in colour, and, on exposure to air in a thin layer, dried in a "day at the ordinary temperature, giving a varnish-like residue. On heating in a " water oven at 100 deg. C. the oil dried and formed a resin-like solid. 113 "The 'constants' of the oil were determined and found to agree well with those "recorded for commercial samples of T'ung Oil, as shown by the following table:— Oil from seeds of Aleurites Fordii. Commercial Tung Oil. "Specific gravity at 15 deg. C. 0.9404 0.9360 Milligrams of potassium hydroxide required per gram of oil. "Acid value 2.89 — "Saponification value - 191.8 152.211 "Iodine value 166.7 149-165.7 per cent. "Hehner value 94.6 96.0-96.6 „ „ "Titer test 42.42.5 deg. C. 37.1-37.2 deg. C. "The examination has shown that the oil extracted from these seeds of Aleurites "Fordii is similar in composition to the T'ung oil of commerce. It is, however, "lighter in colour, and produces a lighter coloured varnish on drying, so that it is "probably a purer product. It is noteworthy also that it has a higher titer test "than commercial T'ung Oil." It was fortunate that no fatal case occurred amongst the children who had eaten these nuts. But for the prompt action of the Police in getting into communication with the City Authorities, and thus enabling me to secure the remainder of the consignment, there is every probability, as the following day was a Bank Holiday, similar cases of poisoning would have occurred on a larger scale. The Sanitary Committee, recognising the intelligence and valuable assistance given by the Police, directed that they be thanked through the Commissioner for the services rendered to the public health on this occasion. In view of the fact that the vendor of these nuts had offered them for sale in entire ignorance of their dangerous character, no legal proceedings were taken against him, but the Committee marked their sense of the impropriety of his having sold them solely on the strength of the word of a stranger that they were of an edible variety, by cautioning him to exercise greater care in this direction in the future. The Southwark Authorities subsequently took action against him for selling nuts, which were unwholesome and unfit for food, to the costermonger, and against the latter for exposing them for sale, the dealer being fined £5 and £2 2s. costs. The summons against the costermonger was withdrawn. 114 * MEAT SUPPLIES AT THE CENTRAL MEAT MARKET. Although deliveries of meat to the Central Market do not quite reach the record of 1906, they still show a weight some 1,500 tons in excess of any previous year, and although the falling off in the quantity actually condemned as unfit for human food is satisfactory as showing some improvement in methods of transit, yet it could hardly be contended that the quality of meat on which the population of London lives, is not making retrograde movements year by year. Why this should be, the reasons for it, and the prospect for the future, are subjects which may well form matter for consideration in this year's report, in the hope that, from our experience in the past, may be gathered wisdom which shall guide us in the future. The supply to the Central Market comprises:— (а) English Town killed. (b) British Country killed, including Scotch. (c) Foreign killed (i.e. Continental, &c.) (d) American Home killed. (e) American and Argentine refrigerated. (f) Australian, New Zealand, and Argentine frozen. (a).— Taking them in this order, the town-killed meat consists of beef from the Metropolitan Cattle Market, Islington, pigs consigned to the slaughtermen there, and when killed sent to Smithfield to be sold, sheep and lambs purchased by carcase butchers, and after slaughter, marketed here. The British cattle trade has been in an unsatisfactory condition throughout the year, the cold season has not conduced to the fattening of the animals, and the placing of large army contracts in the hands of the Americans has been productive of results far from encouraging. The Corporation has re-modelled the methods of slaughtering at the Metropolitan Cattle Market, and it is hoped it will attract the productions of some of the better class graziers, and as the United States has sent us some 50,000 fewer bullocks this year than last, there may yet be a future for the English beef trade. The pigs in this category have been throughout of satisfactory quality, though cases of tuberculosis are still distressingly numerous amongst them. The sheep also have been of good quality, very free from disease, and have almost invariably realised satisfactory prices, indeed, mutton has throughout the year been the bright spot of the Home trade. For the remarks on pp. 114-117 I am indebted to Mr. G. P. Terrett, Chief Inspector of Slaughterhouses and Meat. 115 (b).—Country-killed meat has been generally of first class quality. Scotch beef maintains its pre-eminence as being the very best article of the kind in the market, and the mutton from the Lothians and border districts has left nothing to be desired. Scarcely, if at all inferior, are the productions of the West Country breeders, while splendid carcases of the Suffolk Down breed from their own and adjacent counties have, from time to time, excited universal admiration. Lincolnshire and other counties have also sent us some excellent mutton, though it must be remembered that there is a largelv increased local consumption, and that here the meat is in competition with that produced all over the world, this and the habit importers have of swamping the market create an element of risk which does not attract to London. (c).—There has been a certain amount of poultry, game, and other small goods from France, Russia, and Italy, but except these, nearly all the supply under this head came from Holland, Denmark, and Belgium. Dutch mutton has mostly been good, and the pork, so far as it was inspected and labelled, excellent, but a large quantity not labelled, of which the quality and soundness was just, as it happened, has also been sent, and it is greatly to be regretted that there should be any delay in enforcing the law as to the compulsoryinspection of all carcases for export to this country. There has been rather less of mutton, while the supply of Dutch pork has at no time been excessive, and the prices have ruled somewhat high, probably the high price of meat in Germany has something to do with this, and also the fact that so little meat of any kind has come from Denmark. The imports from Belgium are limited to pork and rabbits, of the former there is a certain method of inspection wholly unreliable, and not carried out in accordance with the standard laid down in the recommendation of the Royal Commission, while the latter for the most part reach the consumers without coming into the City. (d).—We now come to a very important branch of this subject, viz., cattle imported alive and slaughtered at Deptford, Birkenhead and elsewhere, the two places named furnishing almost the entire supply of this article for London. A few years ago these were great gaunt animals of any age, "which means certainly aged," now they are chiefly neat, symmetrical bullocks of 2, 3, or at most 4 years old, carefully brought over and landed with an immunity from bruises which is nothing short of remarkable, after having been sufficiently rested from the voyage to fit them for slaughter, they dress into neat bright carcases of beef greatly resembling the best Scotch, and well calculated if it were so desired, to deceive buyers into the belief that they were getting that article; the illusion would, however, probably breakdown under the knife and fork test. It scarcely needs demonstration that cattle prepared with so much skill and cost must realise a fairly high price for the trade in them to be remunerative, the shortage of 90,000 bullocks is probably the effect of (a) greater requirements to feed the ever increasing population of the United States, (b) the low 116 prices ruling here as a consequence of the abundance of low priced refrigerated beef from Argentina, and (c) the badness of the season in Canada, "which caused the imports therefrom to be few in number and indifferent in quality, as compared with previous years. Of sheep imported alive, the number was small, while the quality left much to be desired ; in this connection, when it is considered that Argentine sheep are regularly shipped to Antwerp, slaughtered there, and then carcases, skins, fat, &c., sent on here, one cannot help wondering what is saved by the process ; the splendid sheep market at Deptford, probably the best in the kingdom, stands nearly idle, there they would come under the supervision of the officers of the Corporation, and would be prepared by British labour for consumption by the British public. (e).—Beef is brought in refrigerating chambers (a) from the United States, chiefly Chicago, (b) from Argentina. The first has been coming to this country for more than 30 years, and has got to be looked upon as quite a standard article on the British Market ; it is for the most part excellent beef, the length of time it has passed in the cold chamber has been just enough to make it ready for cooking; it has, for the last two years or so, had to face a very keen competition from the Argentine article, and some of the importers have made the mistake of thinking that competition was a question only of price, they have therefore shipped an inferior article for which they were prepared to take a low price, and so have, to some extent, sacrificed the reputation of their own meat; others have continued to send the best they could get, and now find themselves standing on a different level. The condition of this beef has been almost invariably good throughout the year, and the quality furnishes little room for comment beyond the remark made above. Argentine refrigerated beef is the object to which all eyes are turned, because probably the development of this trade contains the secret of the beef supply of England for many years to come. Taken generally, the meat has been more carefully handled, and the arrival of supplies better timed, than previously, but much of it has been poorly selected, badly prepared for slaughter, and not well handled on its arrival here ; this last is owing to the fact that most of the importers being people who have been engaged in the frozen meat trade, have taken for granted that like it, refrigerated meat could be dallied with indefinitely, whereas the three weeks or so occupied on the journey is about the utmost it will stand, and it should, on its arrival here, be brought into consumption as quickly as possible. However, one of the largest works has just passed into the hands of a Chicago firm, and however undesirable that may be from a British point of view, there is no doubt it will lead to the meat being more rapidly placed in the hands of the consumer. Another matter which may possibly have a bearing on this branch of the trade is the introduction of the Linley system of preserving beef in sterilized air. The s.s. "Guardiana" was specially fitted up for this system, and landed her beef here in excellent condition. It would be unsafe to generalise 117 from one shipment, but so far as we have seen, the merit appears to be in the immunity from prejudicial change after leaving the refrigerator, thus differing entirely from ordinary refrigerated beef, which, in this humid climate, deteriorates with great rapidity. Further shipments will show whether there is any real advantage in this system. (f).—Importers of frozen meat from New Zealand and Australia have also had very keen competition from the various companies bringing frozen meat from Argentina. There has been an increased supply of sheep from Australia. Most of the carcases have been good useful mutton, but a great many were fat and wasteful, while some of the lambs and young sheep were very imperfectly made up, and thawed out wet, so that it would be easy to mistake them for dropsical ones. There has also been an abundant supply of sheep and lambs from New Zealand, but although these still retain their superiority to any other frozen produce, they are not as good as formerly. No doubt they have to be sought for further afield, but whatever the reason may be, we miss the symmetry and fullness of flesh of the old Canterbury, for which the North Island and others, which have been sent to make good the deficiency, are inadequate substitutes. Meanwhile the breeders in Argentina are finding some results from the use of the splendid rams they have purchased in Shropshire and elsewhere in this country, and have been sending us a class of mutton which is improving year by year, and bids fair in a short time seriously to challenge the claim of New Zealand to the premier position. Of frozen beef, New Zealand and Australia have both sent us considerably more than previous years, having in that way contributed to make good the shortage of American live cattle. The New Zealand beef has been good, and they appear now to have learned the lesson of careful preparation for slaughter, as there has been very little loss from "bone taint." The quantity of frozen Argentine beef has been about on a level with previous years. It may here be remarked that this branch of the trade is getting very largely into the hands of the Americans and a few others, whose arrangements for distributing meat of this kind are widespread in character; hence the business is done more largely than ever from dock stores and other depots of a similar description, ignoring Smithfield even for many of the larger London buyers, thus emphasising the necessity for a closer and more rigorous inspection of those stores containing as they do almost unlimited means of concealing anything which the proprietors may wish to screen from observation; they are for the most part in the hands of people who would not lend themselves to any improper proceeding of this kind, but that does not in any measure relieve a public body of its duty to keep them under observation. The other food imported in a frozen state consists of American pigs, pork loins and cuttings, boned quarters of beef, sheeps' and pigs' plucks, kidneys, and the bye-products of slaughtering, as to which there is nothing to add to the comments of last year. Their method of sending boneless chucks 118 and scrap meat has been adopted by Argentine, New Zealand and other shippers. The whole subject is now being dealt with by the Local Government Board, and the objections to its importation are fully discussed on pp. 125-132. Three Assistant Inspectors have been added to the staff during the year. The following naval officers have received instruction in the examination of meat, viz.:—The Assistant Director of Victualling, Whitehall ; seven Fleet Paymasters; nine Staff Paymasters; ten Paymasters ; one Assistant Paymaster, and a Fleet Paymaster of the Imperial Japanese Navy, to all of whom, with two exceptions, certificates of competency have been issued. In four instances the senders of bad meat were prosecuted, convictions being obtained in each case. This is the smallest number for many years. For details see p. 138. DISEASED MEAT WITH VETERINARY CERTIFICATE. On the 9th January, 1907, a consignment of 14 pigs, sent by a farmer at Northolt, Middlesex, was received by a salesman in the Central Market, and six of them found by your Inspectors to be tuberculous were consequently seized and destroyed. Accompanying these pigs was a Certificate signed by the County Veterinary Inspector stating that he found them "free from symptoms of contagious or infectious disease, and fit for human food," and that he had therefore authorised their removal. On the following day four other pigs from the same farm accompanied by a similar Certificate from the same Veterinary Inspector arrived at the Market, and of these three were condemned as tuberculous, and one as being in a generally inflammatory and congested condition. These were consequently destroyed as being unfit for food. A third case of this kind occurred, when 11 carcases of pigs, out of a consignment of 45 sent to the City Market, were found to be tuberculous, and therefore seized and destroyed. These pigs were accompanied by a permit signed by an officer of the Huntingdon Police authorising their removal under the Swine Fever Order. Upon enquiry it was found that the permit was granted on the strength of a Certificate from a Veterinary Surgeon that the pigs were "perfectly free from either a contagious or infectious disease and fit for human consumption." 119 In each of these cases the farmers had acted in good faith, having asked a Veterinary Inspector or Police Officer to have the carcases examined before removal. The question, however, arose, why the Veterinary Surgeon in each instance certified diseased carcases to be "fit for human consumption." The reason given by one was that tuberculosis is not a disease scheduled by the Board of Agriculture, and that certifying officials have no power to detain any animal or carcase affected by it. The other Veterinary Surgeon concerned stated that after examining 52 carcases of pigs, together with the lungs and livers, in which organs he should expect to find tuberculosis, he passed 45 of them perfectly free from either contagious or infectious disease, and that opinion he must adhere to. These explanations being extremely unsatisfactory, the Sanitary Committee ordered the facts to be reported to the Board of Agriculture and Fisheries, who, under date the 11th April, furnished the following explanation:— It appears that under Article 4 of the Swine Fever Order, 1894, Rule 2 : "Carcases of swine shall not be removed from an infected place except "with the permission in writing from an Inspector or Officer of the "Board or an Inspector of the Local Authority." In the cases in question, the owners of the pigs were desirous of moving some carcases of swine from an infected area ; in order to conform with the above rule it was necessary to obtain the required certificate. In neither case was the Veterinary Surgeon required by Rule 2 of the Order to do more than give permission in writing for the removal of the carcase from a Swine Fever infected place. The point is, that in each of these instances, although the carcases in question were affected with tuberculosis, the Veterinary Inspector had certified that they were fit for human food. In these circumstances I recommended that the Board of Agriculture be requested to draft a form of Certificate that would obviate any misapprehension in future—this suggestion being made in view of a statement contained in the Board's communication that they were considering the Order referred to. 120 MARKING OF MEAT: In the Annual Report for 1906 it was pointed out that when the new Public Abattoir, established by the Corporation to take the place of the private slaughterhouses that had hitherto existed at the Metropolitan Cattle Market at Islington, under the control of the Corporation, was opened, an excellent opportunity would be presented of initiating a system of marking meat, that on inspection was found to fulfil the requirements of the Local Government Board recommendations with regard to meat inspection. Several reasons were advanced for making this suggestion. Stated briefly, they were as under:— (i.) The promotion of the interests of British Agriculturists. (ii.) When purchasing marked meat the public is guaranteed that not only are they buying British produce, but also that the meat is unquestionably wholesome. With regard to (i.), considerations of space prevent any enlargement on this point, but some figures given and arguments advanced on pages 121 and 122 of the Report for 1906, referred to above, should command serious attention. In June, 1907, I recommended to the Sanitary Committee that the Cattle Markets Committee of the Corporation should be invited to give this suggestion their consideration. This course was agreed to, and in September following, the Cattle Markets Committee reported that although concurring in the view that marking of meat, if general, would greatly benefit Agriculturists, they were of opinion that the time was not yet ripe for introducing the principle. The matter is still under consideration. SUGGESTED MEAT MARKET DEPOT AT CENTRAL MARKETS, SMITHFIELD. In October, 1907, in introducing this subject to the Sanitary Committee, I adverted to the question of Meat Marking at Islington—referred to above — and to the opinion expressed by Mr. King, Veterinary Surgeon to the Corporation, to the effect that he agreed with the principle of meat marking, and that such marking would greatly benefit Agriculturists, also that there is little doubt that a great deal of foreign meat is sold as Home produce. 121 The suggestion referred to was that the time appeared opportune for the Corporation to offer facilities for marking meat that could be brought to a depot established for that purpose in the Central Market. The system would of course be a voluntary one, and a nominal charge made for inspection and marking which might cover the expenses incurred. The Depôt would be placed in charge of an experienced officer, and a record kept of all meat examined. This should specially appeal to the trade, as it would offer protection against any charge of unsoundness that might be brought against the salesman by other authorities, while at the same time it would protect the salesmen against unjust claims for return of money on the ground that articles purchased were unfit for food at the time of sale. It is not unlikely that sooner or later the Corporation will be placed under some official obligation with regard to marking of meat passing through markets under their control, and it seems advisable, therefore, to attempt some experiments in the direction indicated, to demonstrate the extent to which the marking of meat would be received both by the trade and by the public. UNSOUND POULTRY. Attention was given last Autumn to the best means of reducing the amount of game and poultry condemned in the City Markets. In the Annual Reports a table is given classifying the meat seizures during the year, and under the heading "Poultry" is included also game and rabbits. This class of food is particularly liable to undergo decomposition in warm weather, especially when improperly packed or stored, and in the case of poultry to wrong treatment before killing. This loss being mainly preventable, and due in a great measure to lack of method and ignorance, it was suggested that it would serve a useful purpose,, and check to some extent the destruction of much valuable food, if salesmen in the City Markets were supplied with circulars to send to their country customers, containing information as to the best methods of preparing, sorting and packing such goods for market. The Sanitary Committee having concurred in this proposal, notices containing concise directions for these purposes were furnished to all game dealers in the City. A copy of the notice is appended. 122 Corporation of London PUBLIC HEALTH DEPARTMENT. NOTICE. UNSOUND POULTRY, GAME, etc. THE Corporation of London has recently had under consideration the very large amount of Poultry and Game condemned in its Markets, the value of which amounts on an average to several thousand pounds per annum. Enquiry shows that a large proportion of this waste is due to carelessness or ignorance of consignors as to the best methods of preparing and packing such goods. Poultry and Game require for their preservation in good condition the greatest possible care and attention to details at every stage, of killing, storing, and packing. The Corporation has therefore requested its. Medical Officer of Health to prepare the accompanying Circular Letter on the subject. Copies may be obtained for distribution on application. JAMES BELL, Town Clerk. Public Health Department, Guildhall, E.G., December, 1907. 123 Hints as to the proper methods of preparing and packing Poultry and Game for Market 1.-KILLING. (a) Birds intended for slaughter must be kept for a certain time without food. Twenty-four hours is the shortest time that should be allowed for "fasting," and this should be increased in hot weather. If killed without proper fasting, the flesh will not keep properly, and will not look so well. (b) The greatest care must be taken to prevent damage to the skin and tissues of the bird,—bruised flesh rapidly decomposes. The simplest plan of killing is by dislocating the neck. The birds should at once be placed on shaping boards, head downwards. Plucking should be commenced before the flesh is cold. Game should be hung up or laid out separately until quite cold. 2.-PACKING. Poultry and Game must be allowed to become quite cold before packing. To this end each bird should be laid out separately in a cool place, if possible in a direct current of air. More loss is due to inattention to this important detail than to any other cause. In sending Poultry to the London markets there is a prevalent practice of hurriedly dispatching imperfectly cooled consignments to be in time for market. The cooling will be carried out while "shaping." Poultry and Game should always be packed in such a way as to receive as much fresh air as possible. The packages must be freely ventilated. Hampers and baskets may be made sufficiently open to allow of ample circulation of air. If wooden cases are used, they should either be crates made of battens, with wide spaces, which are useful and inexpensive, or, if boxes, openings should be made in the tops, bottoms and sides. The material used for lining the cases and filling in between the birds should be light and porous, and the packing, while loose enough to allow of ventilation, should be sufficiently firm to prevent bruising of the flesh. Thoroughly dry wheat (not oat) straw is the best material for packing. It is certain that, if the above points are carefully attended to, Poultry and Game will arrive at market in far better condition than at present, and much preventible destruction of good food will be avoided. The above hints have been drawn up after consultation with some of the leading salesmen in the Market, whose valuable co-operation is hereby acknowledged." WILLIAM COLLINGRIDGE, M.D., Medical Officer of Health, City of London. 124 PUBLIC HEALTH (REGULATIONS AS TO FOOD) ACT, 1907. This Act was brought in by the Government in July, 1906, but was not then proceeded with. It was re-introduced in February, 1907, received the Royal Assent on the 28th August of that year, and became immediately operative. This Act enables the Local Government Board to make Regulations for the prevention of danger arising to public health from the importation, preparation, storage and distribution of articles of food. This object is attained in the Act by extending the power of the Local Government Board to make Regulations under the Public Health Act, 1896, and enactments mentioned in that Act so as to make provision for:— (a) The examination and taking of samples of articles of food or drink (other than drugs or water) intended for human consumption. (b) Applying, as respects any matters to be dealt with by the Regulations, any provision in any Act of Parliament dealing with the like matters, with the necessary modifications and adaptions. (c) Providing for the recovery of any charges authorised to be made by the Regulations or any services performed thereunder. In this Act the machinery existing with regard to epidemic diseases under the Public Health Act, 1875, the Public Health Act, 1896, and the Public Health (London) Act, 1891, is utilised for the purpose of food inspection. When this measure was under consideration, there was a feeling in some quarters that it placed too wide a control in the hands of a single minister; the Government accepted this view, and added Section 2, by which no regulations made under the Act can become operative until they have been laid forty days before Parliament. The Local Government Board having some time past had under consideration the issue of such regulations, the Court of Common Council, on the 31st October, 1907, agreed to a Report of the Sanitary Committee submitting, on the recommendation of your Medical Officer of Health, that the attention of the Board be directed to the importance of making Regulations under the Act for the following purposes:— (1) For preventing the admission of any meat, or meat products, into this country that have not been officially examined and certified as wholesome before export, the inspection to be in accord with the standard in force in this country at the time. At present the only standard existing is that laid down in the recommendations of the Royal Commission on Tuberculosis, 1898. 125 (2) For ensuring that all imported tinned meat should be properly sterilised and bear the date of packing and the name of the packer or his registered mark or number impressed on the tin. (3) For prohibiting the conveyance of meat through the public thoroughfares, except in properly constructed carts or other suitable vehicles in which the contents are properly covered and protected. CERTAIN IMPORTED MEAT FOODS OF QUESTIONABLE UNWHOLESOMENESS. The Local Government Board recently issued a Report of their Inspector of Foods, Dr. G. S. Buchanan, containing notes on the following subjects:— (1) Imported Boneless Scrap Meat, (2) Imported Pork, in regard to Tuberculosis, (3) Imported Tripe, Tongues, and Kidneys, which are heavily dosed with boron and other preservatives, and making suggestions for dealing with the above-mentioned classes of imported food. Dr. Buchanan points out that the matter was brought to the notice of the Board by questions asked in the House of Commons and by representations from the Corporation. "PART I.—IMPORTED BONELESS SCRAP MEAT- "The notes on this subject are arranged as follows :— " 1. Nature of imported boneless scrap meat; countries of origin; amount " imported; uses of this material. "2. Inspection in regard to boneless scrap meat. "3. Objections to imported boneless scrap meat. "4. Suggested application of Regulations to imported boneless scrap meat. "1. Nature of Imported Boneless Scrap Meat ; Countries of Origin; " mount Imported; Uses of this Material. "Boneless Scrap Meat from the United States of America." Boneless Beef. Most of this comes from the United States, a small quantity prepared with preservatives, the greater part frozen. These goods are a bye-product of the canning trade—that is, they are composed of the inferior parts of inferior animals, the wholesale price being about 3d. a pound. This meat is generally used by makers of meat pies and sausage makers, only a part of it reaches the 126 Central Meat Market, and the boxes are often sold unopened direct from various Cold Air Stores, and in some cases no Market tolls are paid. The total importation of boneless beef chucks of all descriptions during 1905 into the United Kingdom was 2,722 tons. Boneless Pork. This meat which comes from the United States of America is composed chiefly of "pork cuttings" and "ham trimmings," these are mainly made up of the scraps which are removed in preparing the hams and other parts of the carcases. The total quantity of pork and ham cuttings and butts imported during 1905 was rather over 800 tons. Boneless Scrap Meat from Argentina. This entirely consists of beef which provides a certain amount of boneless meat, but not of scrap meat similar to that from the States. Setting aside "box offals," i.e., livers, hearts, kidneys, &c., it consists mainly of skirts, ox-cheeks and ox-tails. Boneless Scrap Meat from Australia, New Zealand and Canada. Only a small proportion of this finds it way into London. Small quantities of beef and pork trimmings preserved with boric acid are imported from Canada. Boneless Scrap Meat from the Continent of Europe. "Nothing is known of this by the Inspectors of Smithfield Market, with the "exception of some salted boneless beef from Denmark. It is stated, however, that "there is a trade in sausage meat of a dubious character from different Continental "ports, which does not find its way into the public markets. " 2. Inspection in regard to Boneless Scrap Meat. " Inspection in this Country. "Inspection in this country can afford no check whatever as to the existence of "disease conditions in the animals whence meat of this description is derived. Nor "can any such inspection operate as a check upon the use of uncleanly or "objectionable methods of obtaining, treating, collecting or packing the scrap meat "in the place of its preparation abroad. Dr. Collingridge has drawn special "attention to this matter in reports to the City Corporation, and it has been insisted "upon by the Medical Officers of Health of Birmingham and other cities where "there are important meat markets. "An Inspector, however skilled, can make nothing of chucks or trimmings while "they are frozen hard, and all he can do in practice, when they thaw, or after they "have thawed, is to look for signs of decomposition. Inspection for decomposition is of "itself a matter of some importance, as the chief use of the scrap meat is in the "mincing machine, and it is obviously undesirable that scraps which are in doubtful "condition should be allowed to be turned into sausage meat. 127 "Inspection in the Country of origin. "It is doubtful whether any system of inspection, however rigid, can in practice "be regarded as satisfactory in the case of meat prepared for export in the form of "scrap meat. Meat in this form is rightly held in considerable suspicion by health "authorities. In the experience of this country it has been found specially "convenient to traders wishing to make profitable use of bad or doubtful meat. "Certain foreign meat laws take account of this objection to scrap meat of all kinds. "In the German Empire the importation of the kind of scrap meat here referred to 'is interdicted. In the case of the boxed boneless chucks, trimmings, &c., now "chiefly in question, it has to be remembered that the meat may be of inferior "quality to begin with (canners). Moreover, preparation of scrap meat must "involve a considerable amount of handling ; scraps may be collected from dirty "floors or thrown into unclean receptacles, and are liable to be dirtied or ''contaminated in other ways. "United States Inspection.—In a memorandum addressed to the Board early in "1906, I drew attention to the law and regulations in the United States as to the "inspection of establishments where animals are slaughtered and meat is prepared "for export. Save for certain questions regarding tuberculosis, referred to below, "the instructions of the United States Bureau of Animal Industry to its inspectors "at these establishments, in regard to their judgment and condemnation of animals ''affected by disease, appeared to constitute a code which could be regarded as "generally satisfactory from the health point of view. I pointed out, however, that "it was quite another question how these regulations worked in practice, and "referred to accounts of the rapidity with which carcases are dealt with in the "large packing houses of Chicago and elsewhere, and to the arrangements for "inspection made there. "Since that date various documents and reports have come to hand in connection "with the agitation which has led to the United States Meat Inspection Act of 1906. "From these it is evident that in the past the staff" of inspectors at the packing "houses must often have been wholly inadequate to secure a thorough inspection of "carcases for disease on the lines of the official instructions. "Until this year, at any rate, the official labels have had no significance as "regards preparation, cleanliness, chemical treatment, and the like, of meat "imported in the form of scraps. "But although, henceforth, the label 'U.S. Inspected' will no doubt mean more "than in the past, it would be rash at this stage to place too great reliance upon its "significance when attached to boxes containing such a commodity as scrap meat "prepared for export." Dr. Buchanan further points out that the standard with regard to tuberculosis is:— "not that which is in force in this country, and that therefore; the home trader has a "legitimate grievance if scraps of pork of tuberculous animals are allowed to come "into the market, for no other reason than that the condition of the animals from "which they are derived cannot be ascertained by inspectors in this country. "Summarising the objections to imported boneless scrap meat, "1. It is meat in an objectionable form. No inspection can suffice to detect disease. 128 "2. It is not prudent to regard it free from suspicion of unwholesomeness, "solely on the strength of the declaration of the importers, or of any "guarantee of inspection which may be furnished by the Government "concerned. "3. In view of the methods of American meat inspection, it is quite "possible it may have been derived from tuberculous animals. "4. It is desirable that sausage makers should obtain meat which is readily "identifiable with particular parts of a carcase, and have some knows "ledge of the meat they use. "5. That boxes of frozen meat have been found to show signs of "decomposition at an early stage after they have been thawed. It is "not desirable that imported sausage meat with unknown and some"times large quantities of chemical preservatives should be sold. "4. Suggested application of Regulations to Imported Boneless Scrap Meat. "The unchecked admission of boneless scrap meat into the United Kingdom "appears to constitute a definite risk to health. It also hinders equitable "administration of public health laws and regulations relating to unsound or "diseased meat in this country. "I have repeatedly found this question of boneless scrap meat referred to by "Home traders when speaking of the hardship of having their meat seized by local "authorities on account of disease or other conditions which rendered it "unwholesome." "At the present time the volume of trade in this commodity is small, and to "prohibit its importation would cause little interference with trade." PART II.—TUBERCULOSIS AND IMPORTED PORK. Dr. Buchanan refers to the Recommendation of the Royal Commission of 1898, "the presence of tuberculous deposit in any degree should involve seizure of the whole carcase and of the organs," and the fact that the Board has advised the adoption of this standard by Medical Officers of Health and Meat Inspectors, and further that the second Report of the present Royal Commission emphasises the need for continuing precautions against the risk entailed to human health by food containing tubercle bacilli of the "bovine" type. Reference is made to the systems of inspection in various countries, which are summarised as follows:— "Classification of Imported Pork in regard to Tuberculosis. "In 1906 I found that imported pork could be classified as follows:— "Class A.—Practically free from tuberculosis. Required to have been submitted to examination by a qualified inspector whose instructions are to reject for tuber"culosis in any degree. "Dutch.—The whole of the Dutch pig carcases which bore labels attesting "Government inspection for export. "Danish.—Practically all pork imported from Denmark 129 "Class B.—Some safeguard against tuberculosis afforded by the system of meat "inspection established by the meat law of the country of origin. Such inspection "would exclude pigs affected by tuberculosis in certain forms, but would pass other "tuberculous pigs. "United States Practically the whole importations from these two "Belgium. countries. "Class C.—No safeguard against tuberculosis in any degree. "Dutch.—Pig carcases from Holland not bearing labels attesting Govern"ment inspection for export. "Class D.—Unclassified ; importation small. "Australian, Canadian, Russian, New Zealand, and other countries. "On the Customs figures above given the importation into the United Kingdom "of fresh and frozen pork in these different classes appeared to be approximately:— "In Class A, about 54 per cent, of the total. ” B, ” 34 ” ” ” C ” 9 ” ” ” D 3 ” ” "If suitable action is taken under the new meat inspection law in the Netherlands, "Dutch pigs in Class C may be expected in future to diminish in number or to "disappear altogether. The recent Belgian circular in regard to exported pork has "presumably already effected some transference of Belgian pigs from Class B to "Class A. It may here be noted that the Canadian Meat and Canned Food Act, "1907, provides for systematic inspection of meat foods prepared in Canada for "export, under which regulations will no doubt be made regarding tuberculosis and "other diseased conditions of animals slaughtered for food. A system of official "inspection of meat prepared for export has already been developed in Australia and "in New Zealand. “United States.—As matters now stand, if the label ‘U.S. Inspected’ on "packages of box pork from the United States is accepted at its maximum value, "it affords a guarantee that the meat does not come from animals suffering from certain severe forms of tuberculosis, but does not attest that none of the meat is "derived from tuberculous pigs ; indeed, the United States regulations justify the "assumption that some of the box pork comes from animals affected by tuberculosis." Practical Difficulties in Administration with regard to Tuberculosis in Imported Pork. Dr. Buchanan points out that inspection at the place of slaughter is the only safe way of dealing with pork. The present arrangements are unfair to the Home trader, inasmuch as American boxed pork is allowed to be sold when it may have been derived from tuberculous animals ; the Home trader has no such privilege. He does not suggest that any relaxation should be permitted from the standard set up by the Royal Commission of 1898, but that measures should be taken to secure the greater wholesomeness of that which is imported, and for that purpose suggests the following conditions:— "(a.) Pork imported as carcases to be required to consist of entire carcases "including the head and lymphatic glands about the throat. 130 "(b.) Pork imported in portions less than the entire carcase to be enclosed "in boxes, barrels, bags, or other receptacles bearing an official mark "which has been accepted by the Board. The Board's acceptance of "any proposed mark as an official mark would depend upon the "evidence forthcoming from the exporting country that the mark "affords a guarantee that the carcases from which the portions of pork "have been derived hare been examined by competent and responsible "officers and have been found free from tuberculosis in any degree. PART III. TRIPE, TONGUES AND KIDNEYS, HEAVILY DOSED WITH PRESERVATIVES. This deals with the question of the addition of boron compounds and other preservatives for the purpose of preservation. A statement is made that the amount found is seldom less than 60 grains per lb., and sometimes over 150 grains per lb. (2.14 per cent.).* "These amounts are so large that it is justifiable to infer that many persons who eat "tripe of this kind would incur definite risk to health from the dose of boric acid "taken. A quarter of a pound of this tripe—which may be considered a moderate "meal—must often contain more than the maximum dose of boric acid prescribed "for an adult by the British Pharmacopoeia. It must be remembered that tripe is "considered to be a food easy of digestion, and is given to invalids on that account. "A further and important objection to this tripe is that decomposition may set in, "but owing to the presence of the preservative, no obvious sign of it will be present. " Suggested Application of Regulations to Tripe, Tongues, and Kidneys "Imported in Preservative Solutions or Packing. "The considerations above set out indicate the desirability of action by the Board, "under the Public Health (Regulations as to Food) Act, 1907, to prevent the "introduction from abroad at English ports of tripe (whether cooked or uncooked), "tongues, and kidneys, which arrive in receptacles containing any preparations "consisting of or comprising boric acid ; borax or other borates; sulphurous acid "or sulphites." It will be noted that the views and recommendations set forth in Parts I. and II. of the above Report strongly confirm the views of the Corporation as set forth in the Reports of the Sanitary Committee. If regulations are made on these lines under the powers of the Public Health (Regulations as to Food) Act, 1907, a considerable safeguard to the health of the people will be established, and a legitimate grievance of the Home trader and producer as to unfair competition will be removed. * In this connection it may be mentioned that it has been found by Professor Klein that—provided the conditions as to locality, general cleanliness, character of the meat and bread are satisfactory—the minimum quantity of "boric preservative" required for arresting decomposition in sausaee meat for a period of three days under normal conditions is 26.24 grains, or 0 37 per cent, of preservative to the pound of sausage material 131 AMERICAN BOXED MEAT. Several cases occurred last year that have a direct relation to Dr. Buchanan's opinion quoted above that:— "Although henceforth, the label ' U.S. Inspected' will no doubt mean "more than in the past, it would be rash at this stage to place too great "reliance upon its significance when attached to boxes containing such a "commodity as scrap meat prepared for export." The new Regulation known as B.A. (Order No. 157) concerning meat inspection of the United States Department of Agriculture came into operation on 25th July, 1906. On the 15th May, 1907, a quantity of plucks taken from a box bearing the stamp indicating that the contents had been "inspected and passed," were seized as being diseased. This was by no means an isolated case, and your Inspectors not infrequently find this label unreliable. Another reason for taking exception to the importation of this boxed meat under present conditions is shown by the fact that a sample taken from a case containing roughly chopped fore-quarter of beef bearing the U.S. label, was found on examination by your Public Analyst to contain 0.78 per cent, of boric acid, equivalent to 54.6 grains per pound, an excessive amount. The Public have a right to know what it is they are buying, and in my opinion, when a preservative is added to any article of food or drink, the fact should be made known to the purchaser by label or otherwise. There is a further danger to health that may arise from the consumption of this boxed meat when packed under insanitary conditions, which evidently prevail at some packing establishments. Early in September, 1907, the attention of Dr. Chalmers, Medical Officer of Health for Glasgow, was directed to a consignment of American boxed meat which had reached Glasgow direct. Upon examination nothing was discovered suggesting unsoundness in the flesh of the animals at the time of slaughter, but micro-organisms were present whose normal habitat is the intestines. By way of explaining their presence in the centre of a mass of frozen meat, two explanations were advanced. In the first place the organisms in question, which belong to the coli group, rapidly penetrate the tissues of animals after slaughter, and so might reach those portions which were contained in the consignment. This, however, from the condition of the flesh otherwise, was considered unlikely, the probable explanation being that due care was not exercised in protecting the surface of the flesh from direct contamination. The practical result is that the flesh containing organisms of this character rapidly undergoes decomposition on thawing, and that the presence of intestinal organisms in flesh food is apt to induce changes therein which experience has shown to be associated with ptomaine poisoning. 132 DISEASED CARCASES BEARING U.S. LABEL. Illustration of the little value of the U.S. label attached to carcases is shown in the following cases. On the 3rd May, 1907, a firm of salesmen in the Central Market, Smithfield, sent from a cold air store to a customer at Reading a fore-quarter of American beef bearing the inspection label of the U.S. Department of Agriculture. Upon arrival at Reading this meat was examined by an Inspector of that authority who found the sternal glands to be diseased. On the 22nd October, 1907, I submitted to the Sanitary Committee specimens cut from the carcase of a pig badly affected with acute pleurisy which had seriously affected the condition of the animal. The carcase, which was in consequence quite unfit for food, was seized and destroyed with the consent of the salesmen. This pig was seized on the 18th October at one of the premises of one of the largest meat importing Companies. Upon enquiry it was found that it came from the State of Massachusetts, U.S.A., in the s.s. "Georgian" from Charlestown. At my suggestion the attention of the United States Government was called to the matter in order that they might have an opportunity of furnishing some explanation, but up to the time of writing this has not yet been supplied. These cases emphasise the necessity of still exercising a most careful supervision over all meat coming from the United States. The new regulations for meat inspection in the United States, though perhaps in themselves commendable, appear to be carried into effect with so little regard to efficiency that your Inspectors would not be justified in passing a carcase bearing a U.S. label without examination. TUBERCULOUS PIGS FROM RUSSIA. In April, 1907, a consignment of 60 frozen Russian pigs, without heads, reached the Market for a preliminary examination while still in a hardened condition; evidence of tuberculosis was found in four out of five, an arrangement was therefore made that in order to facilitate inspection, the whole consignment should be dealt with by one form, when a careful examination disclosed that 13 out of the 60 were tuberculous. Being informed that a large consignment of 250 was expected, and knowing that the Russian standard of inspection was not in accordance with those recommended by the Local Government Board, and adopted in the City Markets, the Sanitary Committee, acting on my advice, directed that the facts be brought to the notice of the Russian Consulate and to the Local Government Board. 133 Table showing the annual deliveries and other details during the past 34 years:— MEAT SUPPLIES, 1874 TO 1907. Section 47 (8) of the Public Health (London) Act, 1891, provides that— "Where a person has in his possession any article which is unsound "or unwholesome or unfit for the food of man, he may, by written "notice to the Sanitary Authority, specifying such article, and containing "a sufficient identification of it, request its removal, and the Sanitary "Authority shall cause it to be removed as if it were trade refuse." This provision takes away the ground of a common defence in prosecutions for being in possession of unsound food. It has often been suggested that the food was set aside with the intention of destroying it before it was discovered by the Inspector. This power to notify to Sanitary Authorities under this section affords a test where this line of defence is adopted. 142 SALE OF FOOD AND DRUGS ACTS. It will be seen from Table I. that in the course of the seven years 19011907, 7,540 samples have been taken in the City, or upwards of 1,000 samples annually, and that of these, 580 were found adulterated; the average annual percentage of adulterated samples during the seven years being 7.7. The actual percentage of adulteration at the present time is, however, lower than this, having fallen from 13.11 in 1901 to 6.68 in 1907. 144 From the Table it would appear that the decrease of adulteration in the City during the years 1901, 1902, 1903 and 1904 had been followed by an increased amount of adulteration during the three succeeding years 1905, 1906 and 1907. This increase is, however, more apparent than real. There can be little doubt that the numerous prosecutions that have taken place since the Acts were rigorously enforced has brought about a great improvement generally in the character of the food and drugs sold in the City, any apparent increase in the percentage of adulterated articles being due to discrimination in the selection of the articles taken for analysis. In the last published Report of the Local Government Board on the working of the Sale of Food and Drugs Acts, mention is made of the increasing practice of the exhibition of notices stating that articles offered for sale are not guaranteed to be pure or up to standard. Such notices are generally displayed for the express purposes of protecting the vendor from the consequences of selling a sophisticated article, and in order to place difficulties in the way of prosecutions. Such notices are to be found in 21 per cent. of registered purveyors of milk in the City, while notices for the same purpose are exhibited in no less than 40 per cent. of the City Public-Houses. These facts and the advantage taken by unscrupulous traders of the loop-hole afforded them by an easy abuse of the warranty clauses are greatly conducive towards weakening an effective administration of the Food and Drugs Acts. With regard to the Notices exhibited in licensed premises, I think it would be well, when the annual consideration is given to the renewal of licences by the Local Authority in the City, to furnish the Justices with a list of all those houses in which such notices are used. It is obvious that no publican who is carrying on his business honestly and properly need be in fear of a visit from an Inspector, and if the Justices gave due weight to this aspect of the question it might assist them in coming to a decision in those cases where there is any doubt as to the advisability of the renewal of a licence. In some of these cases the samples were taken at the request of a retail milkman to test the quality of the milk supplied by farmers or wholesale dealers. PRESERVATIVES IN MILK. In February 1907 the Sanitary Committee referred to me for consideration and report some resolutions with regard to this subject passed by the Metropolitan Borough Council of Paddington. These were as under:— "(a) That communications be again addressed to the Local Govern"ment Board and the Board of Agriculture urging that statutory effect "be given to the recommendation of the Departmental Committee of "the Local Government Board of 1901, prohibiting the use of any "preservatives in milk. "(6) That, in the event of the Local Government Board being unable "to act in accordance with the foregoing resolution, regulations under "Section 4 of the Sale of Food and Drugs Act, 1899, should be made by 146 "the Board of Agriculture, defining, in accordance with the circular "letter of the Local Government Board of the 11th July, 1906, the "exact amounts of formalin and boron preservatives which may be added "to milk where disclosure of the presence of preservative is made at the "time of sale. "(c) That a copy of the foregoing resolutions be forwarded to the "London County Council, to the Corporation of the City of London, "and to each of the Metropolitan Borough Councils. With regard to (a) I reported that I was in entire accord with the resolution, and I therefore repeated the opinion I had already expressed* that the time had arrived when the recommendations of the Departmental Committee, referred to in the resolutions, should be given a legal value, and adopted by the Board of Agriculture and Fisheries under the provisions of Section 4 of the Sale of Food and Drugs Act, 1899. As to (6), I was unable to advise that either formalin or boron preservative be allowed to be introduced into milk in any degree, and in this view I am supported by the recommendation of the Departmental Committee, viz.:— "That the use of any preservative or colouring matter whatever in "milk offered for sale in the United Kingdom should be constituted an "offence under the Sale of Food and Drugs Act." Resolution (c) required no comment. This report was agreed to, and a copy ordered to be sent to the Paddington Borough Council. IMPROPERLY LABELLED IMPORTED SKIMMED MILK TINS. In January, 1907, it was reported that upon examining the contents of 100 cases, each containing 96 tins, marked " Machine Skimmed Condensed Milk,"—part of a consignment of returned stores from South Africa—delivered at a dealer's premises in the City, it was found that although the cases were marked in accordance with legal requirements, the tins within were not. Section 1, Sub-section 1 (c) of the Sale of Food and Drugs Act, 1899, requiring that Condensed, Separated, or Skimmed Milk imported into the United Kingdom in tins or other receptacles should bear a label whereon the words "Machine Skimmed Milk," or "Skimmed Milk," are printed in large and legible type, failing which the importer is liable, on summary conviction, to a fine not exceeding £20 for the first offence, and £50 for a second, and £100 for any subsequent offence. The tins in question were not labelled in accordance with this provision. * See pages 136 and 137, Annual Report, 1906. 147 As the Act provides that offences under this Section shall be undertaken by the Commissioners of Customs, the facts were communicated to them, and in reply thereto, His Majesty's Board of Customs undertook to instruct their officers to take samples from any further importation of condensed milk of the same brand unless the label bears the words "Machine Skimmed Milk," it being the practice of the Board of Agriculture to see that such milk is so labelled in accordance with the Section of the Act referred to above. Such instructions were in due course issued by the Board, and a copy of the same forwarded to the Town Clerk. BUTTER. In the case of the offender who was fined £100, it was the fourth conviction, and evidence was given that he had instructed his assistants never to serve persons who were not regular customers with margarine when butter was asked for, but in serving known persons to do so. As in former years special attention was given to the character of the butter served at coffee-houses, and of the 184 samples taken at these places, of butter served upon slices of "bread and butter," it was found that in 8 cases, or 4.3 per cent., margarine had been used instead of butter.
