No other form of butter sophistication was reported during the year. BUTTER AND MARGARINE ACT, 1907. (7 Edw. VII., Ch. 21). Shortly after the introduction of the so-called "milk-blended butter," about the year 1900, it became apparent that existing legislation did not afford sufficient protection to the public from what one witness, before the Select Committee on the Butter Trade, described as having been originally "solely a fraud, nothing but a fraud, and a deception with the intention of passing off something which was not what it pretended to be." The manufacturers of this article—which is really a debased butter— succeeded in eventually evading the provisions of the Sale of Food and Drugs Act. It is true that proceedings might have been taken under the " Merchandise Marks Acts," but the enforcement of these Acts rests with individuals and not with any public authority. To those not familiar with the nature of this article it may be useful if I quote here the notes made by me on the subject when dealing with the proceedings of the Select Committee's enquiry on the Butter Trade in the Annual Report for 1906. "One of the most important features of the enquiry was the evidence "taken in relation to so-called "milk-blended butter." "This is a form of sophistication that I have already directed attention to in my Annual Reports for the years 1903-4-5. "It was ascertained in the course of the enquiry that this so-called "butter is made in this country by five firms, and it was also given in "evidence that it is composed of butter and milk, with the addition of "boracic acid, that have been mixed together by machinery, one firm "alone making from 80 to 85 tons a week. "Upon its first introduction, some years ago, it was sold as butter, "such description being, of course, a fraudulent one. This abuse was 149 "checked to some extent by the imposition of fines in cases where the "firms manufacturing it were proceeded against under the Sale of Foods "and Drugs Acts, for selling butter containing an excessive amount of "water. "For some reason or other this mixture appealed to a certain section "of the community that did not know butter when they saw it, and who "were readily imposed upon by cleverly worded advertisements. This "stuff is now sold as" milk-blended butter," a title cleverly designed to "mislead. It is further placed in a wrapper, bearing an artfully worded "declaration that purports to describe its real nature. "This statement protects the manufacturer from prosecution, as it is "a sufficient disclosure that the article is a mixture. It professes to be "a "blend" of butter and milk, but the ordinary buyer can hardly be "expected to know that the milk so added itself contains about 87 per "cent. of water as a natural constituent. "Nor do the public know that this " butter," as a result of such "blending, when completed may contain as much as 28 per cent. of "water. "A sample purchased in the City on the 16th May, 1906, was certified " by the Public Analyst for the City to contain the parts as under:— " Butter Fat 64.8 per cent. "Water 28.0 „ " Card 5.7 „ " Salt and Ash 0.8 „ " Boric Acid (H3B03) 0.7 „ 100.0 "It was first placed on the market some seven years ago. If the "recommendations contained in the Report of the Select Committee are "adopted in any new legislation, the sale of this article under the "description of butter will be prohibited." From time to time measures have been introduced in Parliament for remedying this state of things; the first Bill for dealing with "milk-blended butter" being brought in, in 1902. A second Bill was introduced in 1903, other Bills followed in 1904 and 1905, these being on the lines of the 1903 Bill as amended in Committee. 150 On the 5th March, 1906, the Select Committee before referred to was appointed "to consider whether any, and if so, what further legislation was required in order to secure the better control and conduct of the trade in butter and butter substitutes." The result of their deliberations was ordered to be printed on 9th July, 1906, and a summary of the same with a copy of the recommendations of the Committee will be found in my Annual Report for that year. The Butter and Margarine Act, 1907, is the result of the Select Committee's Report. It was introduced in the Commons as a Government measure by the Representative of the Board of Agriculture and Fisheries on the 17th February, 1907, and received the Royal Assent on the 21st August of that year. The Act came into operation on the 1st January, 1908. The object of this Act is to prevent the adulteration of butter at its source instead of, as in previous Bills, making the seller or retailer responsible. It will bring under efficient Government inspection those Factories and traders who for years past have been sophisticating butter. The Act provides for the inspection of all so-called butter factories where blending is done, as well as all margarine factories, and a thorough system of tracing the products from one wholesale dealer to another until it reaches the retail trader. The penalties for infringement of the Act will probably act as a deterrent, as the fines vary from £20 for a first offence, £50 for a second offence, and not exceeding £100 for the third and any subsequent offence, and in cases where imprisonment can be inflicted under Section 17 of the Sale of Food and Drugs Act, 1899, to such imprisonment as is by that section authorised, i.e.: "(2) Where, under any provision of the Sale of Food and Drugs "Acts, a person guilty of an offence is liable to a fine not exceeding "£50, and the offence in the opinion of the Court was committed "by the personal act, default, or culpable negligence of the person "accused, that person shall be liable (if the Court is of opinion that "a fine will not meet the circumstances of the case) to imprison"ment, with or without hard labour, for a period not exceeding "three months." Provision is made in the Act for the registration of factories and ments, also for the inspection of factories by Officers of the Board of Agriculture and the Local Government Board, and by an Officer of the Local Authority, who is authorised to procure samples under the Sale of Food and Drugs Acts, if specially authorised in that behalf by the Local Authority, and he shall have the same powers of entry, inspection and sampling as regards any premises registered with the authority as a butter factory. It will be an offence under the Act if any substance intended for use 151 for adulteration of butter is found in any butter factory, and the occupier of such factory shall have committed an offence if any oil or fat capable of being used for adulteration is found on the premises. A limit of 16 per cent. of moisture in butter and margarine and 24 percent. in milk-blended butter is fixed. In section 5 there is included a list of Articles, the importation of which is made an offence by section 1 of the Sale of Food and Drugs Act, 1899. There are:— "(e) Butter containing more than 16 per cent. of water. "(f) Margarine containing more than 16 per cent. of water. "(g) Any butter substitute containing more than 24 per cent. of water. "(A) Any butter substitute or imitation butter, except in packages "conspicuously marked "Butter Substitute" or "Imitation "Butter," as the case may require, or with such name as may be "approved by the Board of Agriculture and Fisheries for the "purpose." Under section 6 the Board of Agriculture has power to make regulations with regard to the proportion of any curd in any samples of butter, and under section 7 the Local Government Board may make regulations as to prohibition for use of all preservatives that may be specified in the regulations in the making of butter, margarine or milk-blended butter, or for limiting the extent to which any particular substance or preservative may be used in the manufacture of such articles. The Act does not prohibit the sale of milk-blended butter, but it must be dealt in under certain restrictions. It can only be manufactured for sale or sold to the public under a name to be approved by the Board of Agriculture and Fisheries, and under the conditions applicable to the sale or description of margarine, with the substitution of an approved name for the word "Margarine," except that in any case where, in order to comply with those conditions the article is delivered to the purchaser in a wrapper, there shall, in addition to the approved name, be printed on the wrapper in such manner as the Board may approve, such description of the article, setting out the percentage of moisture or water contained therein as may be approved by the Board. The foregoing are the main provisions of the new Act, but it should be pointed out that it is largely legislation by reference, and the time has arrived when it would be to the advantage of those who have to administer the Food Adulteration Acts, as well as to the public generally, if the several enactments dealing with the question were consolidated in one Act. 152 The following are the new duties imposed upon the Corporation by this Act:— (1.) The registration of butter factories. (2.) The registration of any premises on which there is manufactured any milk-blended butter, or on which there is carried on the business of a wholesale dealer in milk-blended butter. (3.) The inspection of and sampling at any premises registered in the City as a butter factory. With regard to (3), Officers of a Local Authority can only have powers of entry, inspection or sampling in butter factories when specially authorised in that behalf by the Local Authority. For these purposes, therefore, my recommendation that your Medical Officer of Health and the six Senior Sanitary Inspectors should be authorised in accordance with Section 2 (2) of the Act, was agreed to at the Court of Common Council on the 31st October, 1907, and the Local Government Board and Board of Agriculture and Fisheries notified accordingly. NETHERLANDS BUTTER BILL. In January, 1907, I submitted for the consideration of the Sanitary Committee the views of the Public Analyst for the City—Dr. Teed—with regard to the suggested Netherlands Butter Bill, a synopsis of which the ConsulGeneral of that country had been good enough to furnish. Briefly, the Bill contained provisions for the control of places where butter is made, on much the same lines as obtains in the English Butter and Margarine Bill passed last year, but as there were certain clauses in it of a techno-chemical character, the Committee invited Dr. Teed's opinion thereon. Dr. Teed's notes on the Bill were as under :— "The first Clause 'Commercial Butter must contain a certain per"centage of fat, to be fixed by Royal Decree,' is somewhat similar to "Clause 4 of the Sale of Food and Drugs Act, 1899, which gives power "to the Board of Agriculture to fix certain standards for butter (as also "for milk, cream, and cheese). So far as regards butter, the Board of "Agriculture has only fixed a standard as to the amount of water it "may contain, but they could also fix a standard for fat if they chose. "The second Clause, 'Margarine shall not indicate a higher saturation "figure than 10 for the volatile fatty acids contained in it,' means that "the amount of butter permissible to be mixed with margarine shall "not exceed some 40 per cent. As it stands, no method of anal) sis 153 being indicated, and the statement referring to margarine, and not to "fat of margarine, it is quite possible that some dealer who is proceeded "against under it, might persuade the Court that it had no meaning. "What I understand is, 'The fat of margarine shall not indicate "a higher saturation figure than 10 for the volatile fatty acids contained "in it by the Reichert Meissel process of analysis.'' "In the Sale of Food and Drugs Act, 1899, Section 8, the amount of "butter fat in margarine fat is limited to 10 per cent. It seems a pity "that the standard could not be arranged to be identical in the two "countries." The Committee concurred in my view that Dr. Teed's observations were of sufficient importance to be brought to the notice of the Netherlands Government, especially the last paragraph advocating international agreement as to the permissible amount of butter in margarine. This course was adopted, and on 25th March, 1907, the Consul-General for that country wrote to say that he had received a request from the Netherlands Department of Agriculture to convey their thanks to Dr. Teed for the interest he had shown in the matter, and was desired to point out that the figures mentioned in the Clauses to which Dr. Teed had referred applied to butter as well as to margarine, to molten and filtered fat; further, that the opinion was expressed in a review of a synopsis of the Bill only. With regard to the progress of this measure, the Consul-General of the Netherlands informs me that under present political conditions the Government that came into office quite recently will have to decide whether it will take over the Bill from its predecessors. When the magnitude of the figures relating to imported butter are considered, it will be seen that any legislation in any country, exporting that article to the United Kingdom, dealing with the control of manufacture and the precautionary measures observed for safe-guarding its purity, must be of special concern both to the trade and to the public. As mentioned elsewhere in this report, our own manufacturers of butter can now only carry on their business under special restrictions to ensure the purity of their products, and unless the maker of butter imported from abroad is under similar control, the public must suffer, and the home producer be placed at a disadvantage. In this connection the following figures are not without interest. Twenty-two samples were taken, one of which was reported against. In this instance cotton seed oil was served as a substitute. It should be borne in mind that this refers only to olive oil sold as such. There is no definition of so-called "Salad" oil, which may be composed of any oils provided they are not actually poisonous. 155 The most common substitute for olive oil is cotton seed oil, and there is little doubt that the increased use of this oil is largely responsible for the troubles of digestion so common at the present day. Apart from its value in the preparation of salads, fish, &c., olive oil is a wholesome, nutritive article of diet, especially useful in the case of children and persons unable to digest ordinary fat. DRUGS. The accompanying Table shows the extent of drug adulteration in the City for the last seven years:— Samples taken. Found genuine. Found adulterated. Percentage of adulteration. 1901 111 103 8 7.2 1902 79 74 5 6.3 1903 65 60 5 7.7 1904 52 48 4 7.7 1905 61 56 5 8.2 1906 47 42 5 10.6 1907 65 59 6 9.2 Metropolis (1906) 5.0 I have had occasion in previous Reports to point out that the proportion of drugs found to be not up to standard requirements was far too high, and little improvement in this respect has taken place last year. It is not suggested that any fraud is intended on the part of the vendors, but that the offence is due to carelessness or neglect to ensure that the drugs sold are in accordance with the recognised standard. Included in the adulterated samples were two of powdered gentian root containing 25 per cent. and 40 per cent. respectively of ground olive stones. Both offenders were prosecuted. In each case, however, the drug had been in stock for a number of years, and had been purchased by the vendors with the business. Each was ordered to pay 12s. 6d. expenses. Two samples of light magnesia were reported against. One, an informal sample, was certified to contain 20.9 per cent. of carbonic acid and water; the other, a formal purchase at the same shop, 11.5 per cent. of carbonic acid and 7.7 per cent. of water. The vendor of the latter was prosecuted and fined £5 and 17s. 6d. expenses. In a case where iron pill was asked for, the purchaser was supplied with quinine and iron pill. This was an informal sample. Samples taken subsequently at the same shop were returned as genuine, thus showing that the sale of pills containing quinine in the first instance was an illustration of gross carelessness. 156 PRESCRIPTIONS. It is obviously of the greatest importance to the public generally, and the medical profession in particular, that prescriptions should be made up with absolute accuracy, yet analysis showed that in 25 cases of simple prescriptions handed over the counter, no less than 13, or 52 per cent., of them were found to be inaccurately dispensed. Upon enquiry it was found that in many cases the deficiency of active principles was due to a careless system of dispensing which was becoming common, viz. : the correct quantity of the drug was measured or weighed, but the water was simply added in amount sufficient to fill the bottle, the capacity of which was assumed to be accurate. That this assumption is a dangerous one is shown by the fact that it was found by experiment that a six-ounce bottle may be as much as 5½ drams, or 11.25 per cent. in excess of the reputed contents. Consequently, if the assumed capacity of the bottle is relied upon by the dispenser, and the dose measured, the quantity of the drug ordered by the physician to be taken by the patient will be diminished to this extent. The only way, therefore, in which accuracy in dispensing can be ensured is to measure the amount of water as well as the drugs. It being clear that in these cases there was no intention of fraud or intention to deceive on the part of the chemists, no legal proceedings were taken against them, and, at my suggestion, the Committee directed that a circular letter, of which a copy is here given, should be sent to every prescribing chemist in the City. "DISPENSING. "Several samples of prescriptions recently made up at Chemists' shops "in the City of London have been certified by the Public Analyst to be "deficient in the ingredients required by the formula submitted by the "purchaser. "Investigation shows that in some cases the deficiency of the active "principle prescribed was due to the prevalent custom among dispensers "of weighing or measuring the drug and adding sufficient water to fill "the bottle, the capacity of which is assumed to be accurate. This is a "dangerous assumption, as shown by the fact that a sample of reputed "six-ounce bottles was found to contain on an average 5½ drams in "excess of this quantity, equal to 11.25 per cent. It is obvious that if "the dose be measured, the quantity of the drug taken by the patient "will be diminished to this extent. "Accuracy in dispensing is essential, and this can only be ensured by "measuring the water employed as well as the drugs. 157 "The cases referred to having been reported to the Sanitary Com"mittee of the Corporation of London, I have been instructed to direct "your attention to the importance of this point, which, if not carefully "observed by yourself and your assistants, may render you liable to "proceedings under the Sale of Food and Drugs Acts. As a result of these proceedings some firms in the City now affix a special label to all bottles of medicine compounded by them, of which the following is an example:— "N.B.—In conformity with the new requirements of the Board of "Health Department of the City of London, this Prescription has been "accurately measured into the bottle ; rendered necessary on account "of the inaccurate fluid capacity of bottles. It is suggested that a "graduated glass measure should at all times be used to correctly "measure the dose. Aug. lst, 1907. The only exception that can be taken to this label is that the requirement is no new one, and the words "requirements of Pharmacopoeia Britannica" should be substituted for the words "new requirements of the Board of Health Department of the City of London." Later in the year it was found necessary to take proceedings against several chemists for not making up prescriptions in accordance with the formula asked for. These resulted in the imposition of three fines of £5, and one of £2, with 17s. 6d. expenses in each instance. In none of these cases was it suggested that the offenders attempted in any way to defraud the purchaser, and the prosecutions were instituted solely with a view of protecting the public from the ill effects likely to be caused from carelessness in dispensing, and to induce chemists to take the requisite means for ensuring that the stock preparations and drugs are exactly what they are represented to be by the wholesale manufacturers from whom they obtain their supplies. In one case where a mixture made from a prescription which ordered Iodide of Potassium and Tincture of Cinchona was certified to be deficient in cinchona alkaloids to the extent of 18 per cent., the wholesale dealer subsequently acknowledged that the article they had furnished the chemist with was not up to standard. Such cases, however, in no way relieve the dispensing chemist from his obligations to the public and the medical profession, he is supposed to have the necessary knowledge to be able to test his stock, or he can take the precaution of purchasing all tinctures, &c., under a warranty, which latter is a good defence in any proceedings that may be taken against him. In commenting on the prosecutions referred to above, it has been pointed out in an article in the Pharmaceutical Journal on "Inaccurate Dispensing," that although up to the present, faults in compounding have been dealt with by 158 Local Authorities under the Sale of Food and Drugs Acts, it is also an offence under Section 15 of the Pharmacy Act of 1868, which enacts that:— "Any chemist who shall compound any medicines of the British "Pharmacopoeia, except according to the formularies of the said Pharma"copoeia, shall for every such offence be liable to pay a Penalty or sum of "Five Pounds." The writer of the article referred to states that:— "Local Authorities acting on the reports of their analysts may even "persuade the Privy Council to move the Pharmaceutical Society to "take action. Proceedings could only be instituted by the Registrar, "and it will be noted that medical practitioners, apothecaries and patent "medicine mongers cannot be hit for the foregoing offence." Although the Pharmaceutical Society has not had occasion to exercise the powers which it has in this connection, it must be borne in mind that such powers have not lapsed, and that if in consequence of habitual carelessness on the part of compounders the Council is called upon to exercise powers conferred upon them by Section 26 of the Act referred to, it will be seen that the consequences may be of much gravity to the offender. This Act provides that:— "The Privy Council may direct the name of any person who is con"victed of any offence against this Act, which in their opinion renders "him unfit to be on the Register under this Act, to be erased from such "Register, and it shall be the duty of the Registrar to erase the same "accordingly." It is a curious fact that in the existing Sale of Food and Drugs Acts no allusion whatever is made to the British Pharmacopoeia, and it is probably due to the reference to that authority in Section 15 of the Pharmacy Act, 1868, before quoted, that for many years it had been customary for magistrates to accept it as a standard in dealing with cases of drug adulteration. It was not until the year 1901 that it was definitely decided in the High Court that:— "If a drug to be found in the Pharmacopoeia is asked for, this drug "must be supplied, and that if it is not sold with the ingredients and in "the proportions prescribed by the Pharmacopoeia, there is at least "primd facie evidence that what is sold is not of the nature, substance, "and quality which was demanded." The above is an extract from the judgment of the Court delivered by Mr. Justice Phillimore in the case of Dickins v. Randerson in the King's Bench Division on the 29th January, 1910. 159 This was an appeal by case stated from the decision of Justices of the West Riding of Yorkshire convicting the appellant, a Pharmaceutical Chemist, upon an information preferred against him by the respondent, an Inspector of Food and Drugs, charging him with unlawfully selling, to the prejudice of the respondent, two ounces of mercury ointment, which on analysis was found to be deficient in mercury. It was contended on behalf of the appellant that he was not by law required to sell mercury ointment to a purchaser in accordance with the standard quality prescribed by the British Pharmacopoeia, except when specially asked by the purchaser to do so, or when making up the prescription of a medical practitioner : and that accordingly it was within the discretion of the appellant to supply the diluted preparation in all cases in which he thought fit to do so, and that he had therefore committed no offence under Section 6 of the Sale of Food and Drugs Act, 1875. The Justices found that the commodity sold was not of the nature, substance, and quality of the article demanded by the purchaser, and prescribed in the British Pharmacopoeia, whether expressly asked to do so or not. The questions for the opinion of the Court were:— (1) Whether the appellant was so bound as last aforesaid or not. (2) Whether there was any evidence to support the finding of the Justices. (3) Whether the information was properly laid under Section 6 of the Sale of Food and Drugs Act, 1875. (4) Whether the conviction was good in law. The conviction was affirmed. (This important case was fully reported in "The Times" of 30th January, 1901.) In accordance with the Standing Order of the Court that no person shall enter the employ of the Corporation or any of its Committees without previously passing a medical examination to the satisfaction of the Committee having cognisance of the appointment, 50 persons were so examined during 164 1907, and of these 7—or 14 per cent.—were reported by me as physically unfit for office. In conclusion, I desire to express to the Sanitary Committee my appreciation of much kindly consideration extended to me during the past year, and to recognise as fully as possible the loyal support and hearty co-operation of the staff by whom, of necessity, the greater part of the actual work has been carried out to the entire satisfaction of the Corporation. I have the honour to be, Gentlemen, Your obedient Servant, Medical Officer of Health for the City of London. Public Health Department, Guildhall, E.C.
